Razi's Two-Pronged Response: Solitary Hadith and Age Calculation
Razi refutes objections by challenging hadith certainty and calculating Ali's age.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi refutes objections by questioning the certainty of solitary hadith and using age calculations to establish possibility.

Core Idea
Razi uses logical possibility to counter historical objections.

In this passage, Razi responds to objections concerning the precedence of Abu Bakr's submission over Ali's. He first dismisses the key hadith as a solitary report that does not provide certainty. Then, he challenges the claim that Ali was a child at the time of his submission by calculating his age to show it was possible he was an adult. This lesson examines these two arguments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two objections
Read the passage and note the two objections Razi is addressing: the hadith about Abu Bakr's precedence and the claim that Ali was a child when he submitted.
2
Understand the first response
Razi argues that the hadith is a solitary report (khabar ahad) and does not yield certain knowledge (la yufid al-ilm). Reflect on why this is significant in theological argumentation.
In Sunni kalam, khabar ahad is not sufficient for establishing certainty in matters of belief.
3
Follow the age calculation
Razi calculates Ali's age at the time of revelation by subtracting known periods from his total age. Verify the numbers: 66 (total age) - 53 (23+30) = 13. This shows Ali was about 12-13 years old, an age when puberty is possible.
Razi uses possibility (imkan) to argue for actuality, but the argument is incomplete in the text.
4
Note the incomplete second aspect
The text cuts off after starting the second aspect of the answer. Recognize that the passage is incomplete, so we only have the first aspect of the response to the age objection.
The missing part likely contained additional evidence or argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لأنا نقول : أما الخبر الذي تمسكتم به في إثبات أن إسلام أبي بكر سابق على إسلام علي فهو من باب الآحاد، ولا يفيد العلم. وقوله : إن عليا حين أسلم ما كان بالغا. قلنا : الجواب عنه من وجهين : الأول: لا نسلم أنه أسلم قبل البلوغ، ويدل عليه أن سن علي كان بين خمس وستين سنة، والنبي لو كان قد بقي بعد الوحي ثلاثة وعشرين سنة، وعلي بقي بعد النبي له قريبا من ثلاثين سنة، فإن أسقطنا مدة ثلاث وخمسين سنة من ست وستين سنة بقي ثلاث عشرة سنة، فإذا كان علي ابن أبي طالب وقت نزول الوحي على النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم فيما بين اثنتي عشرة سنة وبين ثلاث عشرة سنة، وبلوغ الإنسان في هذا السن ممكن علمنا أن كون علي بالغا وقت نزول الوحي على النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم أمر ممكن، وإذا ثبت الإمكان وجب الحكم بوقوعه، لما أن روي النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم قال لفاطمة : زوجتك أقدمهم إسلاما وأكثرهم علما ولو قلنا : إنه :،
For we say: As for the report (khabar) which you have clung to in proving that Abu Bakr's submission preceded Ali's submission, it is of the category of solitary reports (ahad) and does not yield certainty. And his saying: that Ali when he submitted was not an adult. We say: The answer to it is from two aspects: First: We do not grant that he submitted before puberty, and evidence for that is that Ali's age was between sixty-five and sixty-six years, and if the Prophet remained after revelation twenty-three years, and Ali remained after the Prophet close to thirty years, then if we subtract a period of fifty-three years from sixty-six years, thirteen years remain. So if Ali ibn Abi Talib at the time of the revelation to the Prophet was between twelve and thirteen years, and it is possible for a human to reach puberty at this age, we know that the fact that Ali was an adult at the time of revelation to the Prophet is possible. And if possibility is established, it is necessary to judge its occurrence, because it is narrated that the Prophet said to Fatima: 'I married you to the one with the earliest Islam and the most knowledge.' And if we say: that he...
Source pages p. 461

Commentary
This passage is Razi's response to the objections. He addresses two main points. First, he dismisses the hadith used to argue that Abu Bakr's submission preceded Ali's, stating that it is a solitary report (khabar ahad) which does not yield certain knowledge (la yufid al-ilm). This undermines the evidential force of the objection. Second, he responds to the claim that Ali was not an adult when he submitted. He gives a two-fold answer. The first is a calculation: Ali was about 65-66 years old at the time of discussion; the Prophet lived 23 years after revelation; Ali survived the Prophet by about 30 years; subtract 53 from 66 yields 13, meaning Ali was about 12-13 at the time of revelation – an age at which puberty is possible. Razi argues that possibility necessitates actual occurrence, especially since the Prophet said to Fatima, 'I married you to the one with the earliest Islam and the most knowledge.' The response is incomplete in the text, but the two aspects – refuting the hadith's certainty and establishing the possibility of Ali's adulthood – are clear.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Razi conclusively proves that Ali was an adult at the time of his submission.
Correction: Razi does not claim certainty; he only establishes possibility (imkan) and uses it to argue for occurrence, but the argument is incomplete.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Razi uses logical possibility to defend a historical claim. Are you willing to accept that what is logically possible might be true, even if it upsets your long-held assumptions about the Companions? What does your resistance reveal about your attachment to certainty? Will you investigate further?

Razi dismisses a hadith as solitary and uncertain. Have you ever uncritically accepted a narrative about Islamic history simply because it was widely repeated? What would it cost you to re-examine that narrative today?

The possibility that Ali could have been an adult at his Islam challenges a common timeline. If it turned out that Ali submitted before Abu Bakr, would your respect for either Companion change? Why are you emotionally invested in this order? Can you separate historical fact from your personal loyalty?

Solitary Reports and Certainty
The author refutes the claim that a hadith proves Abu Bakr's earlier Islam, noting it is ahad.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why kalam scholars reject solitary hadith in establishing matters of historical precedence and how they apply the principle that certainty requires tawatur.

Core Idea
Not every hadith is proof—certainty requires mass transmission.

In theological debates, not all evidence carries equal weight. The opponent here relies on a specific hadith to argue that Abu Bakr preceded Ali in Islam. But the author counters using a foundational principle of hadith criticism: solitary reports do not produce certain knowledge. This lesson shows how that principle is applied.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic source passage and its literal translation once. Notice the structure: first the opponent's argument, then the author's response.
The passage is short but dense with a key kalam principle.
2
Identify the claim and refutation
The opponent claims a hadith shows Abu Bakr's Islam preceded Ali's. The author refutes by stating the hadith is ahad (solitary) and does not yield certain knowledge.
3
Understand the term 'ahad'
Ahad reports are transmitted through a single or limited chain. In kalam, they are not considered probative for foundational matters. Only tawatur (mass transmission) gives certainty.
This principle is used to reject many historical claims in theological debates.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما علي فإن هذا الحديث يقتضي أنه كان له كبوة توقف في قبول الإسلام، وهذا يدل على أن إسلام أبي بكر كان سابقا على إسلام علي. لأنا نقول : أما الخبر الذي تمسكتم به في إثبات أن إسلام أبي بكر سابق على إسلام علي فهو من باب الآحاد، ولا يفيد العلم.
As for 'Ali, this hadith implies that he had a setback and hesitated in accepting Islam, and this indicates that the Islam of Abu Bakr was prior to the Islam of 'Ali. We say: As for the report you have clung to in proving that Abu Bakr's Islam was prior to 'Ali's Islam, it is of the category of solitary reports (ahad) and does not yield certain knowledge.
Source pages p. 461

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its refutation. The opponent argues that a certain hadith shows Abu Bakr preceded Ali because Ali hesitated. The author responds by noting that the hadith in question is ahad, i.e., transmitted through a single chain, and therefore does not produce certainty (ilm). The author is applying the principle that matters of such importance (like precedence in Islam) require tawatur (mass transmission) to be accepted as definitive. This is a common kalam principle.

Key Terms
الحديث
الآحاد
العلم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any hadith, regardless of its transmission, is sufficient to establish a factual claim in theology.
Correction: In kalam, only mass-transmitted (mutawatir) reports produce certainty; ahad reports are not probative in foundational matters.

Checkpoint
Why does the author reject the hadith that suggests Abu Bakr preceded Ali?
A) It is fabricated.
B) It is a solitary report and does not yield certainty.
C) It contradicts the Quran.
D) It is rejected by consensus.

Key Takeaway
A single-chain report cannot establish a matter of historical precedence; only mass-transmitted reports yield certainty.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Are there areas in your life where you have built your conviction on weak evidence, like a single story or rumor? Will you commit to seeking only certain knowledge before forming judgments about others?
?
This lesson shows that even a sahih hadith might not be sufficient for certainty. How many of your beliefs about your own faith rest on assumptions rather than verified knowledge? What will you do today to strengthen the foundation of your certainty?

The First Response: Ali May Have Been Mature at Conversion
The author presents a chronological argument to refute the claim that Ali was a minor at conversion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the author's first response to the objection that Ali was a minor at conversion: a chronological argument that he was likely mature.

Core Idea
Ali's age at conversion can be calculated to show he was probably mature.

The author begins his defense of Ali's status by challenging the factual premise of the objection. Rather than accepting that Ali was a minor, he presents a calculation based on known ages. This lesson examines that calculation and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
The opponent claims Ali was a minor when he accepted Islam, implying his Islam is inferior. This lesson addresses the first response.
2
Follow the calculation
The author uses known lifespans: Ali lived 65-66 years, Prophet after revelation 23 years, Ali after Prophet ~30 years. Subtracting gives 13 years, so Ali was around 12-13 at start of revelation.
The calculation assumes accuracy of these numbers.
3
Recognize the conclusive evidence
Since the Prophet said to Fatima 'I have married you to the foremost in Islam', this proves Ali was mature, because a minor cannot be considered foremost.
This is a theological argument reinforcing the chronological one.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقوله : إن عليا حين أسلم ما كان بالغا. قلنا : الجواب عنه من وجهين : الأول: لا نسلم أنه أسلم قبل البلوغ، ويدل عليه أن سن علي كان بين خمس وستين سنة، والنبي لو كان قد بقي بعد الوحي ثلاثة وعشرين سنة، وعلي بقي بعد النبي له قريبا من ثلاثين سنة، فإن أسقطنا مدة ثلاث وخمسين سنة من ست وستين سنة بقي ثلاث عشرة سنة، فإذا كان علي ابن أبي طالب وقت نزول الوحي على النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم فيما بين اثنتي عشرة سنة وبين ثلاث عشرة سنة، وبلوغ الإنسان في هذا السن ممكن علمنا أن كون علي بالغا وقت نزول الوحي على النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم أمر ممكن، وإذا ثبت الإمكان وجب الحكم بوقوعه، لما أن روي النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم قال لفاطمة : زوجتك أقدمهم إسلاما وأكثرهم علما ولو قلنا : إنه ما كان بالغا حال ما أسلم لم يصح هذا الكلام. الوجه الثاني في الجواب عن هذا السؤال هب أن عليا ما كان في ذلك الوقت بالغاً، لكن لا امتناع في وجود إسلام صبي كامل العقل قبل سن البلوغ، ولهذا المعنى حكم أبو حنيفة بصحة إسلام الصبي على هذا التقدير، فصدور الإسلام عن علي وقت الصبا يدل على فضله من وجهين : أحدهما : أن الغالب على طبع الصبيان الميل إلى الأبوين، ثم إن عليا خالف الأبوين وأسلم، فكان هذا من فضائله. وثانيهما: أن الغالب على الصبيان الميل إلى اللعب فأما النظر والفكر في دلائل التوحيد والنبوة فغير لائق بهم، فكان اشتغال علي بالنظر والفكر في دلائل التوحيد وإعراضه عن اللعب في زمان الصبا من أعظم الدلائل على فضيلته؛ فإنه كان في زمان صباه مساويا للعقلاء الكاملين.
And his saying: 'Ali, when he accepted Islam, was not mature.' We say: The answer to it is from two aspects: First: We do not concede that he accepted Islam before maturity. Evidence for that is that the age of 'Ali was between sixty-five and sixty-six years, and the Prophet remained after revelation twenty-three years, and 'Ali remained after the Prophet nearly thirty years. If we subtract fifty-three years from sixty-six years, thirteen years remain. Thus, if 'Ali ibn Abi Talib at the time of the revelation to the Prophet (peace be upon him) was between twelve and thirteen years, and maturity in this age is possible, we know that 'Ali being mature at the time of revelation to the Prophet (peace be upon him) is a possible matter, and if possibility is established, then its occurrence must be judged, because it is narrated that the Prophet (peace be upon him) said to Fatima: 'I have married you to the foremost of them in Islam and the most knowledgeable of them.' And if we were to say that he was not mature when he accepted Islam, this statement would not be valid. The second aspect in answer to this question: Suppose that 'Ali was not mature at that time, yet there is no impossibility in the existence of the Islam of a minor with complete intellect before the age of maturity, and for this meaning Abu Hanifa ruled the validity of the Islam of a minor on this estimation. So the occurrence of Islam from 'Ali at the time of his youth indicates his merit from two aspects: One of them: The predominant nature of children is inclination toward parents, then 'Ali opposed the parents and accepted Islam, so this was among his merits. And the second: The predominant nature of children is inclination toward play, while contemplation and thought about the proofs of tawhid and prophethood are not suitable for them. So 'Ali's occupation with contemplation and thought about the proofs of tawhid and his turning away from play in the time of his youth is among the greatest proofs of his merit, for he was in his youth equal to complete rational adults.
Source pages p. 461

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer. The opponent claims Ali was a minor when he accepted Islam, implying his Islam was less valid or inferior. The author presents two answers. The first answer challenges the factual premise: Ali may have been a mature adult at the time based on a calculation of ages. The author calculates that Ali was around 13, which is the age of possibility for maturity (bulugh), and since the Prophet married him to Fatima saying he was the foremost in Islam, it becomes necessary that he was indeed mature. The second answer concedes the premise but argues that the Islam of a rational minor is valid and even more virtuous because it contradicts innate tendencies of children (obedience to parents and love of play). So Ali's early faith shows his exceptional virtue.

Key Terms
بلوغ

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What age does the author calculate Ali to be at the start of revelation?
A) 10 years
B) 12-13 years
C) 15 years
D) 18 years

Key Takeaway
The author uses a chronological calculation to argue that Ali was likely mature when he accepted Islam.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider that the Prophet personally vouched for Ali's precedence, do you live with the same conviction about your own faith? Are you willing to let your actions speak of your priority of Islam over everything else?
?
The author uses precise calculation to defend a point of faith. How carefully have you examined your own beliefs with evidence? Is your faith built on solid reasoning or just inherited tradition?
?
If the Prophet said he married Fatima to the foremost in Islam, what does that say about the value of being early and consistent in faith? Does your current commitment reflect that you are striving to be among the foremost, or have you become complacent?

The Second Response: The Validity and Superiority of a Minor's Islam
The author argues for the validity and superior virtue of a minor's Islam using legal and moral reasoning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the author's second response: even if Ali was a minor, his Islam was valid and more virtuous due to his opposition to natural inclinations.

Core Idea
A minor's Islam can be valid and even more virtuous than an adult's.

Having challenged the factual premise in the first response, the author now concedes the point for argument's sake. He argues that even if Ali were a minor, his Islam is not only valid but actually more virtuous because it required overcoming natural childhood tendencies. This lesson explores that argument.

Guided Path
1
Understand the concession
The author says 'Suppose Ali was not mature' to move the discussion forward.
2
Legal validity of a minor's Islam
The author cites Abu Hanifa's ruling that a minor with complete intellect can validly accept Islam.
This establishes the legal basis.
3
First virtue: opposition to parents
Children naturally obey parents, but Ali opposed his parents by accepting Islam, showing his courage and faith.
4
Second virtue: engagement in contemplation
Children prefer play, but Ali engaged in deep thought about divine proofs, showing intellectual maturity.
This makes him equal to complete rational adults.

Response to the Claim that Abu Bakr's Conversion Brought More Strength
The author refutes an objection and proves Ali's precedence from Quran.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Razi refutes an objection regarding Abu Bakr's conversion and establishes Ali's superiority using Quranic proof.

Core Idea
Precedence in good deeds implies superiority, not social status.

This passage addresses a sectarian objection about the benefits of Abu Bakr's conversion compared to Ali's. Imam Razi challenges the assumption that Abu Bakr was respected before Islam, nullifying the distinction. He then proves Ali's precedence from the Quran.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its English translation to get an overview of the argument.
Notice the objection and the refutation structure.
2
Identify the objection and rebuttal
Find the claim that Abu Bakr's Islam brought strength, and see how Razi denies the premise that Abu Bakr was respected before Islam.
The rebuttal hinges on this denial.
3
Understand the Quranic proof
Razi concludes that Ali is superior using Quranic verses about the 'foremost' and those who hasten to good deeds. Reflect on why precedence entails superiority.
This connects to the lesson's core idea.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : حصل بسبب إسلام أبي بكر شوكة ونوع من القوة، ولم يحصل بسبب إسلام علي البتة شيء من القوة. قلنا : هذا الفرق إنما يظهر لو ثبت أن أبا بكر كان محترما موقرًا فيما بين الخلق قبل دخوله في الإسلام، وهذا ممنوع وإذا كان كذلك لم يظهر الفرق الذي ذكرتم، فثبت بما ذكرنا أن إسلام علي كان متقدما على إسلام أبي بكر. وإذا ثبت هذا وجب أن يقال: إن عليّا أفضل من أبي بكر، بقوله تعالى : (وَالسَّابِقُونَ السَّابِقُونَ أُولَئِكَ الْمُقَرَّبُونَ) [الواقعة: ١٠-١١] ولقوله تعالى في مدح الأنبياء : (إِنَّهُمْ كَانُوا يُسَارِعُونَ فِي الْخَيْرَاتِ) [الأنبياء: ٩٠].
His saying: 'Due to the Islam of Abu Bakr, strength and a type of power were obtained, and due to the Islam of 'Ali, nothing of power was obtained at all.' We say: This distinction only appears if it is established that Abu Bakr was respected and honored among the people before his entry into Islam, and this is denied. And if it is so, the distinction you mentioned does not appear. Thus, it is established by what we have mentioned that the Islam of 'Ali was prior to the Islam of Abu Bakr. And when this is established, it must be said that 'Ali is superior to Abu Bakr, by His saying, the Exalted: 'And the foremost, the foremost, those are the ones brought near' (al-Waqi'ah 10-11), and by His saying, the Exalted, in praise of the prophets: 'Indeed, they used to hasten to good deeds' (al-Anbiya 90).
Source pages p. 462

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the objection that Abu Bakr's conversion brought more strength to Islam because of his social standing. The author denies the premise that Abu Bakr was respected before Islam, stating that this is not established. Therefore, the claimed distinction disappears. Then the author concludes that Ali's Islam was earlier, and he is therefore superior based on Quranic verses about the foremost (as-sabiqun) being the closest to God, and the verse praising those who race to good deeds. This shows that precedence in good deeds implies superiority.

Key Terms
شوكة
محترم
السَّابِقُونَ

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Abu Bakr had social prestige before converting to Islam, which made his conversion more beneficial.
Correction: Imam Razi denies that Abu Bakr was respected among the people before Islam, so the claimed distinction is baseless.

Checkpoint
How does the author refute the claim that Abu Bakr brought strength?
A) He argues that Ali brought more strength.
B) He denies that Abu Bakr was respected before Islam.
C) He says the hadith is weak.
D) He says Abu Bakr's Islam was later.

Key Takeaway
Ali's precedence in Islam is established by Quranic verses, refuting objections based on worldly strength.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about your own acts of goodness, do you measure them by the impact they have on others' perception or by the speed and sincerity with which you performed them? Which measure does Allah honor according to this passage?
?
Have you ever assumed that someone's social standing or the visible effect of their actions determines their worth in the sight of Allah? What does the author's refutation teach you about such assumptions?
?
The Quranic verses honor those who hasten to good deeds. In what area of your life are you delaying your good deeds because you worry about not being 'respected' enough beforehand? What step will you take today to hasten in obedience without concern for your social standing?

The Seventh Proof: Love for the Prophet's Relatives
Imam Razi presents the seventh proof based on Quran 42:23.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Razi uses the Quranic command to love the Prophet's relatives (Quran 42:23) as a proof for Ali's superiority over Abu Bakr.

Core Idea
The Quran makes love for the Prophet's family obligatory, and that obligates superiority.

In this passage, Imam Razi presents his seventh proof for Ali's superiority, drawing on the well-known verse from Surah Ash-Shura. The argument is straightforward: Ali is among the Prophet's close relatives, and the Quran commands love for them. Since this love is obligatory for all Muslims, and obligatory love implies virtue, Ali must be superior to those not included in this command.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text once without focusing on translation. Notice the logical structure: a claim, a Quranic citation, and a comparative conclusion.
The passage is concise but packed with reasoning.
2
Identify the two premises
The argument rests on two premises: that Ali is a close relative of the Prophet, and that love for such relatives is commanded by the Quran. Locate these in the text.
3
Understand the conclusion
The author concludes that Ali is superior because obligatory love for him applies to all Muslims, while Abu Bakr does not share this status. Reflect on why this would imply superiority.
This is a theological claim based on the idea that greater love implies greater merit.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة السابعة : لا شك أن عليّا كان من أولي القربى لمحمد صلى الله عليه وسلم وحب أولي القربى واجب ؛ لقوله تعالى : (قُلْ لَا أَسْأَلُكُمْ عَلَيْهِ أَجْرًا إِلَّا الْمَوَدَّةَ فِي الْقُرْبَى) [الشورى: ٢٣] وأما أبو بكر فإنه ليس كذلك، والذي يجب حبه على جميع المسلمين فهو أفضل ممن لا يكون كذلك. الحجة الثامنة: قوله تعالى: (فَإِنَّ اللهَ هُوَ مَوْلَاهُ وَجِبْرِيلُ وَصَالِحُ الْمُؤْمِنِينَ) [التحريم: ٤] والمفسرون قالوا : المراد بصالح المؤمنين علي بن أبي طالب.
The seventh proof: There is no doubt that 'Ali was among the close relatives of Muhammad (peace be upon him), and love of close relatives is obligatory due to His saying: 'Say, I do not ask you for any reward except love for the relatives' (Ash-Shura 23). As for Abu Bakr, he is not like that. And one whose love is obligatory upon all Muslims is superior to one who is not like that. The eighth proof: His saying: 'For indeed Allah is his protector, and Jibril, and the righteous of the believers' (At-Tahrim 4). The exegetes said: By 'the righteous of the believers' is meant 'Ali ibn Abi Talib.
Source pages p. 462

Commentary
This passage lists two independent proofs for Ali's superiority. The seventh proof argues that Ali is from the Prophet's close relatives (ahl al-bayt) and love for them is obligatory by Quranic command (Ash-Shura 23). Since Abu Bakr is not a relative, and obligatory love implies superiority, Ali is superior. The eighth proof cites the verse from At-Tahrim which mentions 'the righteous of the believers' (salih al-mu'minin). The author notes that the commentators interpret this phrase as referring specifically to Ali. This is a common exegetical claim in Shi'i theology, but here presented as a proof.

Key Terms
أولي القربى
المودة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the verse 'love for the relatives' refers to love for the Prophet himself or all Companions.
Correction: The verse explicitly says 'in the relatives' (fi'l-qurba), indicating the Prophet's family, not the general community.

Checkpoint
What is the scriptural basis for the seventh proof?
A) Quran 33:33
B) Quran 42:23
C) Quran 5:55
D) Quran 4:59

Key Takeaway
The seventh proof argues that because the Quran commands love for the Prophet's relatives, and Ali is a relative, Ali is superior to those not included in this command.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This proof says that Allah commanded love for the Prophet's relatives, and that makes them superior. Do you truly feel that love as a divine command, or have you neglected it? What would change in your heart if you took this obligation seriously?
?
The author contrasts 'Ali and Abu Bakr. When you think of these two great figures, do your feelings align with the Quranic command? Are you willing to submit your personal preferences to what Allah has made obligatory?
?
If love for the Prophet's family is required, then how does your daily life reflect that? Do you honor them in your speech, seek knowledge of their virtues, and defend their honor? Or is this just a theoretical belief?

The Eighth Proof: The Righteous Believers Verse
Imam Razi presents the eighth proof based on Quran 66:4 and its interpretation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Razi uses Quran 66:4, interpreted by exegetes as referring to Ali, as a proof for Ali's superiority.

Core Idea
The Quran singles out Ali as the righteous believer, indicating his special status.

Imam Razi presents his eighth proof from a verse in Surah At-Tahrim. The verse mentions 'the righteous of the believers' alongside Allah and Jibril as protectors of the Prophet. Razi notes that the commentators identified this righteous believer as Ali ibn Abi Talib, implying Ali holds a unique rank among the believers.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text for the eighth proof. Notice it is very brief and relies on external exegesis.
2
Understand the verse's context
The verse from At-Tahrim talks about those who support the Prophet. The phrase 'righteous of the believers' (salih al-mu'minin) is the focus.
The author does not provide the full verse, only the relevant part.
3
Consider the exegetical claim
The author states that the exegetes said the righteous believer is Ali. This is a claim that elevates Ali above other believers.
This interpretation is not universally accepted, but Razi presents it as fact.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة السابعة : لا شك أن عليّا كان من أولي القربى لمحمد صلى الله عليه وسلم وحب أولي القربى واجب ؛ لقوله تعالى : (قُلْ لَا أَسْأَلُكُمْ عَلَيْهِ أَجْرًا إِلَّا الْمَوَدَّةَ فِي الْقُرْبَى) [الشورى: ٢٣] وأما أبو بكر فإنه ليس كذلك، والذي يجب حبه على جميع المسلمين فهو أفضل ممن لا يكون كذلك. الحجة الثامنة: قوله تعالى: (فَإِنَّ اللهَ هُوَ مَوْلَاهُ وَجِبْرِيلُ وَصَالِحُ الْمُؤْمِنِينَ) [التحريم: ٤] والمفسرون قالوا : المراد بصالح المؤمنين علي بن أبي طالب.
The seventh proof: There is no doubt that 'Ali was among the close relatives of Muhammad (peace be upon him), and love of close relatives is obligatory due to His saying: 'Say, I do not ask you for any reward except love for the relatives' (Ash-Shura 23). As for Abu Bakr, he is not like that. And one whose love is obligatory upon all Muslims is superior to one who is not like that. The eighth proof: His saying: 'For indeed Allah is his protector, and Jibril, and the righteous of the believers' (At-Tahrim 4). The exegetes said: By 'the righteous of the believers' is meant 'Ali ibn Abi Talib.
Source pages p. 462

Commentary
This passage lists two independent proofs for Ali's superiority. The seventh proof argues that Ali is from the Prophet's close relatives (ahl al-bayt) and love for them is obligatory by Quranic command (Ash-Shura 23). Since Abu Bakr is not a relative, and obligatory love implies superiority, Ali is superior. The eighth proof cites the verse from At-Tahrim which mentions 'the righteous of the believers' (salih al-mu'minin). The author notes that the commentators interpret this phrase as referring specifically to Ali. This is a common exegetical claim in Shi'i theology, but here presented as a proof.

Key Terms
صالح المؤمنين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think 'righteous of the believers' refers to all righteous believers collectively.
Correction: The author reports that exegetes specified it as Ali, thus it is a singular reference.

Checkpoint
According to the eighth proof, who do the exegetes say is meant by 'righteous of the believers'?
A) Abu Bakr
B) Umar
C) Ali ibn Abi Talib
D) The Prophet himself

Key Takeaway
The eighth proof relies on an exegetical identification of Ali as 'the righteous of the believers' in Quran 66:4, suggesting his unique standing.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
If Ali is indeed singled out in this verse, how does that affect your view of his status among the Companions? Are you willing to follow the exegetes here, even if it challenges your prior assumptions? This is a question of intellectual humility.
?
Imam Razi, a Sunni scholar, presents this proof. Does that challenge any stereotypes you held about sectarian divisions? How do you reconcile such evidence with your own theological commitments? This lesson demands that you confront the complexity of Islamic scholarly heritage without easy labels.

Ali's Early Conversion and Its Two Virtues
Razi concedes Ali's minority and extracts two virtues from his early conversion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi argues that Ali's conversion as a minor demonstrates two specific virtues, countering the objection about his age.

Core Idea
A minor's conversion with full intellect can be valid and even more virtuous.

In this passage, Razi addresses the objection that Ali was not yet an adult when he converted. He concedes the possibility but turns it into a proof of Ali's merit. By highlighting how Ali's actions defied natural tendencies, Razi establishes two clear virtues from his early Islam.

Guided Path
1
Read the concession
Read the first part where Razi admits Ali may not have been an adult but argues that a boy with complete intellect can validly convert. Note the reference to Abu Hanifa's ruling.
The Arabic has a garbled phrase but the meaning is clear.
2
Identify the two virtues
Find the two aspects Razi lists: (1) Ali opposed his natural inclination to follow parents, (2) he engaged in reflection instead of play. Understand how these demonstrate virtue.
3
Reflect on the argument
Consider why Razi presents these as proofs of Ali's superiority even at a young age. How does this address the original objection?
This connects to the broader debate about precedence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الثاني في الجواب عن هذا السؤال هب أن عليا ما كان في ذلك الوقت بالغاً، لكن لا امتناع في وجود إسلام صبي كامل العقل قبل سن البلوغ، ولهذا المعنى حكم أبو حنيفة ولا لا لا لانها بصحة إسلام الصبي على هذا التقدير، فصدور الإسلام عن علي وقت الصبا يدل على فضله من وجهين : أحدهما : أن الغالب على طبع الصبيان الميل إلى الأبوين، ثم إن عليا خالف الأبوين وأسلم، فكان هذا من فضائله. وثانيهما: أن الغالب على الصبيان الميل إلى اللعب فأما النظر والفكر في دلائل التوحيد والنبوة فغير لائق بهم، فكان اشتغال علي بالنظر والفكر في دلائل التوحيد وإعراضه عن اللعب في زمان الصبا من أعظم الدلائل على فضيلته؛ فإنه كان في زمان صباه مساويا للعقلاء الكاملين. قوله : حصل بسبب إسلام أبي بكر شوكة ونوع من القوة، ولم يحصل بسبب إسلام علي البتة شيء من القوة". قلنا : هذا الفرق إنما يظهر لو ثبت أن أبا بكر كان محترما موقرًا فيما بين الخلق قبل دخوله في الإسلام، وهذا ممنوع وإذا كان كذلك لم يظهر الفرق الذي ذكرتم، فثبت بما ذكرنا أن إسلام علي كان متقدما على إسلام أبي بكر. وإذا ثبت هذا وجب أن يقال: إن عليّا أفضل من أبي بكر، بقوله تعالى : وَالسَّبِقُونَ السَّبِقُونَ أُولَيْكَ الْمُقَرَّبُونَ ) أولَيكَ الْمُقَرَّبُونَ ﴾ [الواقعة: [الواقعة: ١٠ - ١١] ولقوله تعالى في مدح الأنبياء : إنَّهُمْ كَانُوا يُسَرِعُونَ فِي الْخَيْرَاتِ الله [الأنبياء : ٩٠]
The second aspect in answering this question: Suppose Ali was not an adult at that time, but there is no impossibility in the existence of the Islam of a boy of complete intellect before the age of puberty, and for this meaning Abu Hanifa ruled [؟] the validity of the boy's Islam according to this estimation. So the occurrence of Islam from Ali at the time of youth indicates his virtue from two aspects: One of them: that the dominant tendency in the nature of boys is inclination towards parents, then Ali opposed the parents and submitted, so this was from his virtues. The second: that the dominant tendency in boys is inclination towards play, but reflection and thought concerning the proofs of tawhid and prophethood are not suitable for them. So Ali's occupation with reflection and thought concerning the proofs of tawhid and his turning away from play at the time of youth is among the greatest proofs of his virtue; for he was in his time of youth equal to perfect rational adults. He [objector] says: "Due to Abu Bakr's Islam, might and a type of strength occurred, and due to Ali's Islam nothing at all of strength occurred." We say: This distinction only appears if it is established that Abu Bakr was respected and honored among creation before his entry into Islam, and this is denied. And when it is so, the distinction you mentioned does not appear. So it is established by what we have mentioned that Ali's Islam was prior to Abu Bakr's Islam. And when this is established, it must be said that Ali is superior to Abu Bakr, by His statement: "And the foremost, the foremost, those are the near ones" [Waqi'a: 10-11], and by His statement in praise of the prophets: "Indeed they used to hasten in good deeds" [Anbiya': 90].
Source pages p. 462

Commentary
This passage is the second answer to an objection regarding Ali's age at conversion and a refutation of a claim about Abu Bakr's conversion bringing strength. Razi first concedes the premise that Ali may not have been an adult at conversion, but argues that a minor with full intellectual capacity can validly embrace Islam, citing Abu Hanifa's legal opinion. He then extracts two virtues from Ali's early conversion: firstly, that he naturally inclined to oppose his parents (the typical tendency of boys) but instead opposed them by accepting Islam; secondly, that boys usually incline toward play, but Ali occupied himself with contemplation of the proofs of tawhid and prophethood, making him equal to fully rational adults. Next, Razi addresses the objection that Abu Bakr's conversion brought social strength to Islam, unlike Ali's. He denies the premise that Abu Bakr was respected before Islam, so the claimed distinction does not hold. Thus, Ali's precedence in conversion is proven, and based on Quranic verses about the foremost (sabiqun) and hastening to good deeds, Ali is superior to Abu Bakr.

Key Terms
صبا
التوحيد
النبوة
فضيلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Conversion at a young age is less valuable or invalid.
Correction: Razi argues that a minor with complete intellect can validly convert, and such early conversion can actually demonstrate greater virtue by overcoming natural inclinations.

Checkpoint
What are the two aspects of Ali's virtue that Razi highlights from his conversion at a young age?
A) His bravery and knowledge
B) His opposing parents and engaging in reflection on proofs of tawhid rather than play
C) His lineage and wealth
D) His patience and sincerity

Key Takeaway
Ali's early conversion shows his virtue because he opposed parental influence and preferred reflection on faith over play.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a moment when you followed your parents' or society's expectations instead of standing firm in your faith. How does Ali's opposition to his parents challenge your comfort zone? What is one step you can take this week to prioritize truth over conformity?
?
You have many distractions—entertainment, social media, hobbies. How often do you occupy yourself with deep reflection on the proofs of God's existence and prophethood? Ali was equal to perfect rational adults at a young age. What is one change you can make today to prioritize reflection over play?
?
Ali's virtue was proven by his actions in youth. Have you ever used your youth as an excuse for spiritual laziness? What would it look like for you to be a 'perfect rational adult' in faith right now, and what concrete step will you take to embody that?

Refuting the Objection and Establishing Ali's Precedence
Razi refutes the strength argument and uses Quran to prove Ali's precedence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi refutes the claim that Abu Bakr's conversion brought more strength and uses Quranic verses to prove Ali's superiority based on precedence.

Core Idea
Precedence in faith, not worldly status, determines superiority.

After demonstrating Ali's virtues, Razi addresses the objection that Abu Bakr's conversion brought strength to Islam. He challenges the premise that Abu Bakr was respected before Islam, thus neutralizing the argument. Then he invokes Quranic verses about the foremost believers to conclude that Ali's earlier conversion makes him superior.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
Read the objection: that Abu Bakr's Islam brought might and strength, unlike Ali's. Understand the claim being made.
The objector is likely a Sunni opponent in the debate.
2
Analyze Razi's response
Razi denies the premise that Abu Bakr was respected before Islam. If that is not established, the claimed distinction vanishes. Notice how Razi turns the objection by questioning its foundation.
3
See the Quranic conclusion
Razi then uses Quranic verses about the foremost (sabiqun) and hastening to good deeds to argue that Ali's precedence in conversion makes him superior to Abu Bakr. Reflect on how this argument fits into the larger sectarian discussion.

Seventh Argument: Love of Relatives Mandates Ali's Superiority
Razi argues Ali's superiority via obligatory love for kin.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi uses the obligation to love the Prophet's relatives to argue for Ali's superiority over Abu Bakr.

Core Idea
Obligatory love for kin proves Ali's greater virtue.

This lesson presents Razi's seventh proof, which hinges on a Quranic command to love the Prophet's near relatives. Since Ali is undeniably among those relatives, such love is obligatory for all Muslims, establishing his superiority over Abu Bakr, who does not share that status. The argument is concise yet powerful, showing how scriptural obligation translates into a hierarchy of virtue.

Guided Path
1
Read the original Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text carefully, noting how Razi structures his proof: premise, Quranic evidence, comparison, and conclusion.
Pay attention to the Quranic verse cited.
2
Analyze the logical structure
Identify the four components: (1) Ali is a close relative, (2) love of relatives is obligatory by Quran, (3) Abu Bakr is not a relative, (4) therefore Ali is more virtuous.
Use the guided breakdown to follow the reasoning.
3
Reflect on the key terms
Understand the terms 'أولى القربى' (near relatives) and 'المودة' (love) as used in the verse and the argument.
These terms are central to the proof.
4
Test your understanding
Answer the checkpoint question to confirm you grasp the argument's basis.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة السابعة : لا شك أن عليّا كان من أولي القربى لمحمد صلى الله عليه وسلم وحب أولي القربى واجب ؛ لقوله تعالى : قُل لَّا أَسْلُكُمْ عَلَيْهِ أَجْرًا إِلَّا الْمَوَدَّةَ فِي الْقُرْبَى [الشورى: ٢٣] وأما أبو بكر فإنه ليس كذلك، والذي يجب حبه على جميع المسلمين فهو أفضل ممن لا يكون كذلك.
The seventh proof: There is no doubt that Ali was among the near relatives of Muhammad, peace be upon him, and love of the near relatives is obligatory due to His statement: "Say: I do not ask you for any reward except love for the near relatives" [Shura: 23]. As for Abu Bakr, he is not so. And the one whose love is obligatory upon all Muslims is better than the one who is not so.
Source pages p. 462

Commentary
This passage presents the seventh proof of Ali's superiority. Razi states that Ali is unquestionably among the Prophet's close relatives, and love of those relatives is made obligatory by the Quranic verse: 'Say: I do not ask you for any reward except love for the near relatives' (42:23). In contrast, Abu Bakr is not a close relative in this sense. Since love of Ali is a duty upon all Muslims, Ali must be more virtuous than whoever does not have such obligatory love.

Key Terms
أولى القربى
المودة
الحب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the verse refers to love for all believers or that it is not specific to relatives.
Correction: Razi specifies that the verse commands love for the near relatives (الْقُرْبَى), not all believers, and this obligation applies to Ali but not to Abu Bakr.

Checkpoint
What is the basis of the seventh argument?
A) Ali's bravery in battle
B) The obligation to love the Prophet's relatives, which applies to Ali but not Abu Bakr
C) Ali's knowledge of the Quran
D) Abu Bakr's lack of lineage

Key Takeaway
Razi argues that because the Quran commands love for the Prophet's near relatives, and Ali is such a relative, his obligatory love proves his superiority over Abu Bakr.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If loving the Prophet's near relatives is a Quranic command, have you ever honestly examined whether your heart truly holds that love? Do you find yourself more attached to other figures—perhaps from your own sect or community—than to the family of the Prophet? What one concrete action can you take today to verify and increase that love?
?
The lesson claims that obligatory love implies superiority. Does your own love for the Prophet's family translate into a practical prioritization of their teachings and examples in your daily decisions? Or is it merely a comfortable sentiment that doesn't challenge your lifestyle? Ask yourself: would an outsider see that love in how I speak of them, how I treat others, or what I defend?
?
Contrast the two figures: Ali, whose love is obligatory, and Abu Bakr, who is not included in this specific command. Does this argument unsettle your assumptions about the Companions? Are you willing to let the text challenge your loyalties, even if it means reevaluating deeply held convictions? What would it cost you to follow this reasoning to its conclusion?

Eighth Argument: Ali as the Righteous Believer Who Supports the Prophet
Razi interprets verse 66:4 to show Ali's unique role as a supporter of the Prophet alongside Allah and Jibreel.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi uses Quran 66:4 to argue for Ali's unique status as one of the Prophet's exclusive helpers alongside Allah and Jibreel.

Core Idea
Ali is singled out as the Prophet's helper alongside Allah and Jibreel, showing his supreme rank.

In this lesson, we examine Razi's eighth argument, drawn from Quran 66:4. Razi unpacks the verse to show that 'the righteous of the believers' is Ali, and that the grammatical structure implies he is uniquely among the Prophet's helpers. This passage reveals both the textual basis and the logical reasoning for Ali's high station.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and note the verse reference
Read the Arabic passage once, focusing on the verse citation and the initial claim that commentators identify 'the righteous of the believers' as Ali. Then read the translation to get the full meaning.
The verse is from Surah al-Tahrim, verse 4.
2
Understand the meaning of 'mawla'
Notice that Razi defines 'mawla' as 'helper' (nasir) to ensure a common meaning across Allah, Jibreel, and the righteous. This is crucial for the argument.
Without this definition, the verse could be interpreted differently.
3
Analyze the two aspects of Ali's virtue
Razi lists two ways the verse indicates Ali's virtue: the restrictive function of 'huwa' (He) and the order of mention. Reflect on how each supports the claim.
The first aspect uses grammatical restriction, the second uses rhetorical honor.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثامنة: قوله تعالى: ﴿فَإِنَّ اللهَ هُوَ مَوْلَتَهُ وَجِبْرِيلُ وَصَلِحُ الْمُؤْمِنِينَ [التحريم: ٤] والمفسرون قالوا : المراد بصالح المؤمنين علي بن أبي طالب، والمراد من المولى ههنا هو الناصر ؛ لأن المفهوم المشترك من المولى بين الله وبين جبريل وبين صالح المؤمنين ليس إلا هذا المعنى، وإذا ثبت هذا فنقول: هذا يدل على فضيلة علي لها من وجهين: الأول : أن لفظ هُو في قوله تعالى: ﴿فَإِنَّ اللهَ هُوَ مَوْلَنَهُ ﴾ [التحريم: ٤] يفيد الحصر، فيكون المعنى إن محمدا لا ناصر له إلا الله وجبريل وعلي الا لها ، : ومعلوم أن نصرة محمد صلى الله عليه وسلم من أعظم مراتب الطاعات. الثاني : أنه تعالى بدأ بذكر نفسه وثنى بجبريل وثلث بذكر عليها وهذا منصب عال.
The eighth proof: His statement, exalted: 'For indeed Allah is his protector (mawlahu), and Jibreel, and the righteous of the believers' [Tahrim: 4]. The commentators said: What is meant by the righteous of the believers is Ali ibn Abi Talib. And what is meant by 'the protector' (al-mawla) here is the helper (al-nasir), because the common concept of 'mawla' between Allah, Jibreel, and the righteous of the believers is nothing but this meaning. And when this is established, we say: This indicates the virtue of Ali from two aspects: The first: That the word 'Huwa' in His statement 'For indeed Allah, He is his protector' indicates restriction (al-hasr), so the meaning is: Muhammad has no helper except Allah, Jibreel, and Ali [alone], and it is known that helping Muhammad, peace be upon him, is among the greatest ranks of obedience. The second: That He, exalted, began by mentioning Himself, then seconded with Jibreel, and thirded with the mention of Ali, and this is a high station.
Source pages p. 462

Commentary
This passage presents the eighth proof based on Quran 66:4. Razi explains that commentators interpret 'the righteous of the believers' as Ali ibn Abi Talib. He first defines 'mawla' as 'helper' (nasir) to find a common meaning between Allah, Jibreel, and the righteous. Then he draws two points: First, the word 'Huwa' (He) before 'Allah' implies restriction—the Prophet has no helper except these three. Since helping the Prophet is one of the highest acts of obedience, being included in this exclusive list shows Ali's great virtue. Second, the order of mention—Allah, then Jibreel, then Ali—confers on Ali a high rank by being mentioned in such company.

Key Terms
المولى
الناصر
الحصر
صالح المؤمنين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That 'mawla' in this verse means 'friend' or 'master' in a general sense, which would weaken the argument about being a helper.
Correction: Razi specifies that 'mawla' here means 'helper' (nasir), as this is the common meaning across all three entities mentioned.

Checkpoint
How does the structure of the verse indicate Ali's virtue?
A) It lists Ali before Jibreel
B) The word 'Huwa' limits helpers to only three, including Ali, and the order of mention honors him
C) It calls Ali a prophet
D) It mentions Ali twice

Key Takeaway
The verse 66:4 restricts the Prophet's helpers to Allah, Jibreel, and Ali, and the order of mention elevates Ali to a high rank.

?

If not, what is preventing you from giving them the love and respect that this lesson shows they are due? How can you begin to rectify that today?

The Hashimite Lineage Argument
Razi enumerates two premises for Ali's superiority.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two-premise argument from lineage that Razi uses to support Ali's superiority over non-Hashimite companions.

Core Idea
Ali's Hashimite lineage proves his superiority over non-Hashimite companions.

Razi presents a logical argument based on lineage, using a hadith to establish that the Hashimite clan is the chosen tribe from Quraysh. This lesson examines the two premises and their implications in the sectarian discussion.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully to understand the two-premise structure.
Note that Razi uses the term 'mutawatir' for the first premise.
2
Identify the two premises
Locate the first premise (Ali is Hashimite) and the second premise (Hashimite is better than non-Hashimite).
The first is based on widespread knowledge; the second on a hadith.
3
Analyze the proof for the second premise
Examine the hadith: Allah chose Quraysh from Isma'il's descendants, and Hashim from Quraysh. This establishes the superiority of Hashimites.
The hadith is the key textual evidence for the second premise.
4
Reflect on the sectarian context
Consider why this argument was significant in debates about the caliphate and Ali's status.
This is one of several arguments Razi presents; lineage is not the only criterion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة التاسعة أن عليا والله كان هاشميا، والهاشمي أفضل من غير الهاشمي. والمقدمة الأولى متواترة، والثانية يدل عليها قوله : صلى الله عليه وسلم «إن الله اصطفى من ولد إسماعيل قريشًا، واصطفى من قريش هاشماً».
The ninth proof: That Ali, by Allah, was Hashimite, and the Hashimite is better than the non-Hashimite. The first premise is mutawatir (massively transmitted), and the second is indicated by his statement, peace be upon him: 'Verily Allah chose Quraysh from the descendants of Isma'il, and chose Hashim from Quraysh.'
Source pages p. 463

Commentary
This passage presents the ninth proof, a two-premise argument. First premise: Ali was a Hashimite (from the clan of Hashim). Razi asserts this is known by widespread report (mutawatir). Second premise: A Hashimite is superior to a non-Hashimite, proven by the hadith: 'Allah chose Quraysh from the descendants of Isma'il, and chose Hashim from Quraysh.' Thus, since Ali is Hashimite and Abu Bakr is not, Ali is superior.

Key Terms
هاشمي
اصطفى
متواترة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think lineage alone is insufficient for virtue, rejecting this argument outright.
Correction: Razi presents this as one of many arguments; lineage is a factor but not the only one. The hadith provides a strong basis for the superiority of Hashimites.

Checkpoint

B) Ali is Hashimite and the Hashimites are the chosen of Quraysh, thus superior
C) Ali is a descendant of Isma'il and Isma'il is a prophet
D) Ali is the cousin of the Prophet and cousins are superior

Key Takeaway
Razi's ninth argument relies on Ali's Hashimite lineage and the prophetic declaration that Hashim is the chosen tribe, making him superior to non-Hashimites.

Tenth Argument: The Hadith of Mawla
Razi interprets the hadith to prove Ali's authority and bestness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam al-Razi uses the hadith 'Whoever has me as mawla, Ali is his mawla' to argue for Ali's authority and superiority over the entire community.

Core Idea
Mawla means master, not friend, establishing Ali's authority over all believers.

Imam Fakhr al-Din al-Razi presents the tenth argument for Ali's superiority using a well-known hadith. The key is the interpretation of the word 'mawla'. Razi argues that the Prophet's role as mawla implies mastery, so when the same term is applied to Ali, it must mean he too is master over the community. He supports this by citing Umar's reaction, which shows that even the Companions understood it as authority.

Guided Path
1
Read the hadith and Razi's argument
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice that Razi begins with the hadith and then explains the meaning of 'mawla'.
Pay attention to the logical flow: premise, interpretation, conclusion.
2
Understand the linguistic argument
Razi argues that since 'mawla' for the Prophet means master, the same must apply to Ali in this context. Consider the logical necessity of this interpretation.
The word 'mawla' has multiple meanings; Razi selects the one that fits the context.
3
Examine Umar's testimony
Razi uses Umar's congratulatory words as evidence that the Companions understood 'mawla' as authority, not just friendship.
Umar's statement 'You have become the mawla of every believer' reinforces the interpretation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة العاشرة : قوله صلى الله عليه وسلم من كنت موله فعلي موله ولفظ المولى في حق محمد صلى الله عليه وسلم لا شك أنه يفيد أنه كان مخدوما للكل، وصاحب الأمر فيهم، وإذا كان الأمر كذلك وجب أن يقال في علي : إنه أيضاً مخدوم ونافذ الحكم فيهم، وهذا يوجب كونه أفضل الخلق والذي يدل على أنه يفيد المعنى الذي ذكرناه ما نقل أن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم لما ذكر هذا الكلام قال عمر لعلي : "بخ بخ يا علي، أصبحت مولى كل مؤمن ومؤمنة إلى يوم القيامة".
The tenth proof: His statement, peace be upon him: 'Whoever has me as mawla, then Ali is his mawla.' And the word 'al-mawla' with respect to Muhammad, peace be upon him, no doubt indicates that he was served by all and the master (sahib al-amr) over them. And if the case is so, it must be said concerning Ali: that he is also served and his judgment is executive over them. And this necessitates him being the best of creation. And what indicates that it conveys the meaning we mentioned is what has been transmitted: that the Prophet, when he said these words, 'Umar said to Ali: 'Congratulations, O Ali, you have become the mawla of every believing man and believing woman until the Day of Resurrection.'
Source pages p. 463

Commentary
This passage presents the tenth proof using the well-known hadith: 'Whoever has me as mawla, then Ali is his mawla.' Razi argues that when applied to the Prophet, 'mawla' means 'master' (the one who is served and whose command is obeyed). By logical extension, the same meaning applies to Ali in this statement. Therefore, Ali is also master over the community, which necessitates that he is the best of creation. To support this interpretation, Razi cites the report that 'Umar congratulated Ali saying, 'Congratulations, O Ali, you have become the mawla of every believing man and woman until the Day of Resurrection,' indicating that 'mawla' here means authority, not mere friendship.

Key Terms
المولى
المخدوم
صاحب الأمر
نافذ الحكم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think 'mawla' in the hadith means friend or ally, not master.
Correction: Razi argues that the context and Umar's response show it means master, as the Prophet is master, so Ali is made master over the believers.

Checkpoint

A) By citing Arabic dictionaries
B) By quoting 'Umar's statement that Ali became mawla of every believer
C) By comparing with other hadiths
D) By logical deduction alone

Key Takeaway
The hadith 'Whoever has me as mawla, Ali is his mawla' is interpreted by Razi to establish Ali's authority over all believers, making him the best of creation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
If you were standing at Ghadir Khumm today and heard the Prophet declare Ali as mawla, would you be like 'Umar and immediately congratulate Ali, or would you hesitate? What does your hesitation reveal about your faith?

The Hadith of Station: Ali's Superiority
Razi presents the eleventh proof using the hadith 'You are to me as Aaron was to Moses' to conclude Ali's superiority.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Razi uses the hadith 'You are to me as Aaron was to Moses' to argue that Ali is the best of the Muslim community.

Core Idea
Ali's station to the Prophet mirrors Aaron's station—and the station implies superiority.

This lesson examines the eleventh argument from Imam Razi's 'Arba'in. He cites a well-known prophetic hadith about Ali's station relative to the Prophet, akin to Aaron's station to Moses. By drawing an analogy with Aaron's status among Bani Israel, Razi concludes that Ali must be the best of the entire Muslim community.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text once without analysis to get its rhythm, then read the literal translation. Note the concise structure: hadith, premise, conclusion.
The Arabic is a single sentence with three parts.
2
Identify the two premises
Identify the two premises: the hadith establishing a station of Ali relative to the Prophet, and the claim that Aaron was the best of his nation. Recognize that Razi treats the hadith as a general statement of station, not limited to a specific role.
3
Follow the logical inference
Understand how Razi connects the premises: if Aaron's station included being the best of his people, and Ali holds the same station, then Ali must be the best of his people. This is an analogy (qiyas) in argument form.
The conclusion is drawn using the word 'فوجب' showing necessity.
4
Reflect on the implication
Consider the significance: this argument positions Ali as the most virtuous of the Prophet's community, which has implications for debates about leadership and merit.
This is a key proof in Sunni-Shi'a discussions about the superiority of Ali.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الحادية عشرة : قوله انت مني بمنزلة هارون من موسى» صلى الله عليه وسلم وهارون كان أفضل من كل أمة موسى، فوجب أن يكون علي أفضل من كل أمة محمد.
The eleventh proof: His statement, peace be upon him: 'You are to me in the station of Aaron to Moses.' And Aaron was better than the entire nation of Moses, so it is necessary that Ali be better than the entire nation of Muhammad.
Source pages p. 463

Commentary
This passage presents the eleventh proof, a concise argument from the well-known hadith: 'You are to me in the station of Aaron to Moses.' Razi adds the premise that Aaron was superior to the whole community of Moses (Bani Israel). By analogy, Ali must be superior to the entire community of Muhammad (peace be upon him). The implication is that Ali is the best of all the Prophet's companions.

Key Terms
منزلة
هارون
موسى
أفضل
الحجة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some argue that the hadith only indicates temporary succession (like Aaron deputizing for Moses) and does not imply general superiority.
Correction: Razi interprets the hadith as establishing a full analogy of station (manzila) including rank and virtue, not just a specific role. The premise that Aaron was the best of his nation is then applied to Ali.

Checkpoint
What logical step completes the eleventh argument?
A) Ali was the Prophet's cousin
B) Aaron was the best of Moses' nation, so Ali must be the best of Muhammad's nation
C) Aaron was a prophet, so Ali is also a prophet
D) The hadith shows Ali is the Prophet's successor

Key Takeaway
Imam Razi leverages the well-known hadith of position and a premise about Aaron to establish Ali's supreme merit over the entire Muslim community.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Razi uses the hadith of station to claim that Ali is the best of the nation. Have you ever accepted a belief simply because it was supported by a hadith without carefully examining the reasoning? How can you ensure your beliefs are based on sound evidence?
?
The analogy between Ali and Aaron is central to this proof. Think of a time when you drew an analogy between two situations. Did you check that the similarities truly justify the conclusion? How does this lesson teach you to approach analogical reasoning in matters of faith?
?
This lesson presents a proof for Ali's superiority. If you were personally convinced of this, how would it change your view of the early Muslim community? Would you be willing to reassess your own loyalties based on evidence?

The Twelfth Argument: Brotherhood and Ali's Titles
Razi cites Ali's titles and the Prophet's brotherhood to assert his superiority.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the twelfth argument for Ali's superiority, which combines the Prophet's exclusive brotherhood pact with Ali and Ali's own claims to prestigious titles.

Core Idea
The Prophet's brotherhood with Ali and Ali's self-proclaimed titles assert his unique rank.

In this passage, Imam al-Razi presents the twelfth proof of Ali's superiority, drawn from the event of the Prophet establishing brotherhood among the Companions and from Ali's own testimony. The argument relies on a reported historical action and a narrated statement. Let us examine how these two elements together strengthen the claim of Ali's elevated status.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic text and its literal translation carefully. Notice the two distinct parts: the report of the brotherhood event and Ali's direct speech.
The passage begins with 'الحجة الثانية عشرة' marking it as a distinct proof.
2
Identify the two components
First, identify the brotherhood pact: the Prophet took Ali as a brother. Second, identify Ali's self-proclamation: he claims exclusive brotherhood and two prominent titles.
The titles 'as-Siddiq al-Akbar' and 'al-Faruq al-A'zam' are usually associated with Abu Bakr and Umar respectively.
3
Understand the argumentative force
Consider how Razi uses these two points together. The brotherhood is an external honor given by the Prophet, while Ali's own words assert an internal recognition of his status. Both are presented as evidence.
The argument relies on accepting the narrated traditions as authentic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثانية عشرة : أنه لما آخى بين الصحابة اتخذه أخا لنفسه، أن وروي عليا قال في مواضع كثيرة : " أنا عبد الله وأخو رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم لا يقولها أحد بعدي إلا كذاب، أنا الصديق الأكبر، وأنا الفاروق الأعظم، وأنا الذي يفرق بين الحق والباطل ".
The twelfth proof: That when he [the Prophet] established brotherhood among the Companions, he took him [Ali] as a brother for himself. And it is narrated that Ali said in many places: 'I am the servant of Allah and the brother of the Messenger of Allah, no one says it after me except a liar. I am the greatest siddiq (as-Siddiq al-Akbar), and I am the greatest faruq (al-Faruq al-A'zam), and I am the one who distinguishes between truth and falsehood.'
Source pages p. 463

Commentary
This passage presents the twelfth proof of Ali's superiority. First, Razi notes that when the Prophet instituted brotherhood among the Companions, he chose Ali as his own brother, a unique honor. Second, Razi quotes Ali's own statements in which he declares himself the 'greatest siddiq' (as-Siddiq al-Akbar) and the 'greatest faruq' (al-Faruq al-A'zam), titles usually associated with Abu Bakr and 'Umar respectively. By appropriating these titles, Ali claims a higher rank. He also asserts that no one else can truthfully claim to be the Prophet's brother after him. This self-testimony is presented as evidence of his elevated status.

Key Terms
آخى
الصديق الأكبر
الفاروق الأعظم
أخو رسول الله

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Ali's self-praise is unreliable because it is self-serving.
Correction: Razi presents it as a narrated report, not as a personal opinion; the tradition is accepted as a historical fact.

Key Takeaway

Two Aspects of the Mawla Argument for Ali's Virtue
Razi explains two ways the word 'mawla' shows Ali's virtue.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two ways Razi argues that the word 'mawla' in Quran 66:4 indicates the virtue of Ali.

Core Idea
The word 'mawla' means helper, and the verse highlights Ali's virtue in two ways.

Razi presents a theological proof for the virtue of Ali based on the interpretation of Quran 66:4. He first clarifies that 'mawla' here means helper, then draws two distinct implications that elevate Ali's status. This lesson walks through those two aspects.

Guided Path
1
Understand the meaning of 'mawla'
Read Razi's definition of 'mawla' as 'helper' (nasir) and why this meaning is common between God, Gabriel, and righteous believers.
This lexical clarification is the foundation of the argument.
2
First aspect: restriction via 'Huwa'
Notice how the word 'Huwa' in the verse implies restriction (hasr), so the meaning becomes that Muhammad has no helper except Allah, Gabriel, and Ali.
Helping the Prophet is a high rank.
3
Second aspect: order of mention
Observe the sequence: God first, then Gabriel, then Ali. This ordering indicates Ali's elevated station.
The mention after two supreme beings shows honor.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والمراد من المولى ههنا هو الناصر ؛ لأن المفهوم المشترك من المولى بين الله وبين جبريل وبين صالح المؤمنين ليس إلا هذا المعنى، وإذا ثبت هذا فنقول: هذا يدل على فضيلة علي لها من وجهين: الأول : أن لفظ هُو في قوله تعالى: ﴿فَإِنَّ اللهَ هُوَ مَوْلَنَهُ ﴾ [التحريم: ٤] يفيد الحصر، فيكون المعنى إن محمدا لا ناصر له إلا الله وجبريل وعلي الا لها ، : ومعلوم أن نصرة محمد صلى الله عليه وسلم من أعظم مراتب الطاعات. الثاني : أنه تعالى بدأ بذكر نفسه وثنى ،بجبريل وثلث بذكر عليها وهذا منصب عال.
And the intended meaning of 'mawla' here is 'the helper'; because the common concept of 'mawla' between God, Jibril, and the righteous believers is none other than this meaning. When this is established, we say: This indicates the virtue of 'Ali, having two aspects: First: that the word 'Huwa' in His statement 'Indeed Allah is his Mawla' indicates restriction, so the meaning is that Muhammad has no helper except Allah, Jibril, and 'Ali [؟], and it is known that helping Muhammad (peace be upon him) is among the greatest ranks of obedience. Second: that He, exalted, began with mentioning Himself, then seconded with Jibril, and thirded with the mention of 'Ali, and this is a high station.
Source pages p. 463

Commentary
This passage is a theological proof (istidlal) for the virtue of Ali based on the interpretation of the word 'mawla' in Quran 66:4. Razi first establishes that 'mawla' in this context means 'helper' (nasir), as it is the common meaning shared between God, Gabriel, and believing men. Once this is settled, he presents two aspects of the proof: First, the word 'Huwa' in the verse implies restriction (hasr), so the verse means that Muhammad has no helper except Allah, Gabriel, and Ali. Helping the Prophet is one of the greatest acts of obedience. Second, the order of mention—God first, then Gabriel, then Ali—indicates Ali's elevated rank.

Key Terms
مولى
ناصر
حصر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That 'mawla' in this verse means 'lord' or 'master' instead of 'helper'.
Correction: Razi clarifies that the common meaning between God, Gabriel, and righteous believers is 'helper'.

Checkpoint
According to Razi, what is the common meaning of 'mawla' between God, Gabriel, and the righteous believers?
A) Master
B) Helper
C) Friend
D) Protector

Key Takeaway
Razi uses the linguistic nuance of 'mawla' meaning helper and the verse's structure (restriction and order) to establish Ali's virtue.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Razi shows that helping the Prophet is a great rank. In your daily life, do you prioritize actions that help the Prophet's mission—such as spreading his teachings and defending his honor—over your personal comforts? What is one specific change you can make this week to be a true nasir?
?
The order of mention in the verse places Ali after Allah and Jibril. How does your own hierarchy of love and respect for the Prophet's family compare? Are there other loyalties that you place above the love of Ahl al-Bayt?
?
Razi derives deep meanings from a single word. When was the last time you paused to reflect on the precise wording of a Quranic verse? Will you commit to studying one verse weekly with tafsir to uncover its layers?

Ninth Argument: Ali's Hashimi Lineage Confers Superiority
Razi enumerates a two-premise syllogism for Ali's superiority.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi uses a syllogistic argument based on lineage and prophetic tradition to argue for Ali's superiority.

Core Idea
Ali's Hashimi lineage is a divinely chosen status that proves his superiority.

Imam Razi presents the ninth argument in his series of proofs for Ali's superiority. This argument is a concise syllogism: Ali was a Hashimi, and being Hashimi is superior, so Ali is superior. The argument relies on a widely transmitted historical fact and a prophetic hadith that designates the Hashimi lineage as chosen by Allah. Let's examine the reasoning and its foundations.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage carefully, then the literal translation. Notice the structure: two premises and an implied conclusion.
The first premise is stated as a fact, the second is supported by a hadith.
2
Identify the two premises
Find the two premises: (1) 'Ali was Hashimi' and (2) 'the Hashimi is better than the non-Hashimi.' Understand how each is supported.
Razi claims the first is mutawatir and the second is from a prophetic hadith.
3
Consider the strength of the argument
Reflect on why Razi uses a lineage-based argument. How does the hadith about Allah choosing Quraysh and then Hashim support the second premise?
This argument connects tribal honor to divine election.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة التاسعة أن عليا والله كان هاشميا، والهاشمي أفضل من غير الهاشمي. والمقدمة الأولى متواترة، والثانية يدل عليها قوله : صلى الله عليه وسلم «إن الله اصطفى من ولد إسماعيل قريشًا، واصطفى من قريش هاشماً».
The ninth proof: That 'Ali, by Allah, was Hashimi, and the Hashimi is better than the non-Hashimi. The first premise is mutawatir, and the second is indicated by his statement (peace be upon him): 'Indeed Allah chose Quraysh from the children of Isma'il, and chose Hashim from Quraysh.'
Source pages p. 463

Commentary
This passage is a syllogistic proof (hujja) for Ali's superiority. Razi presents two premises: first, that Ali was a Hashimi (of the clan of Hashim); second, that a Hashimi is better than a non-Hashimi. He claims the first premise is widely transmitted (mutawatir) and the second is supported by a hadith where the Prophet states that Allah chose Quraysh from the sons of Isma'il, and then chose Hashim from Quraysh. This implies that Hashim is the chosen lineage, and thus Ali, being a Hashimi, is superior.

Key Terms
هاشمي
أفضلية
متواتر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument relies on tribal pride or bias.
Correction: Razi grounds the argument in a prophetic hadith that indicates divine choice, not mere tribalism.

Checkpoint
What is the second premise of the ninth proof?
A) Ali was courageous
B) The Hashimi is better than the non-Hashimi
C) The Prophet loved Ali
D) Quraysh is better than all tribes

Key Takeaway
Razi argues that Ali's superiority is established by his Hashimi lineage, which is supported by both historical transmission and prophetic hadith.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever find yourself taking pride in your family, ethnicity, or social status? How does Razi's argument—that Ali's superiority is based on a divine choice revealed through the Prophet—challenge or affirm that tendency? This lesson invites you to examine the true basis of honor in your own life. What will you do today to shift your identity from worldly lineage to the lineage of faith?
?
The Prophet said that Allah chose Hashim from Quraysh. If you are not from that lineage, does this lesson leave you feeling inferior? Or does it push you to seek another kind of 'chosenness'—one based on taqwa (piety) and following the Prophet? Be honest: do you secretly wish you were born into a 'better' family? How can you actively cultivate the qualities that Allah truly values, regardless of your birth?
