Refuting the Claim of Exclusive Superiority from Verse 3:61
Imam Razi addresses and refutes an objection based on verse 3:61.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Razi's companions refute the claim that verse 3:61 proves Ali's exclusive superiority.

Core Idea
The verse includes all, not just Ali.

This lesson examines a key objection raised by some regarding the verse of mubahala (3:61). Some claim that the phrase 'our souls' refers exclusively to Ali, proving his superiority. The author's companions respond by denying exclusivity and asserting that the verse includes others. Let's explore the argument and its refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the structure: an objection followed by an answer.
The passage is short but complete as a unit.
2
Identify the objection
The objection is that verse 3:61 (the mubahala verse) is used as proof for Ali's superiority over others. Find the key phrase 'التمسك في التفضيل'.
3
Identify the response
The response begins with 'فالجواب عنه' and gives two points: first, they do not concede exclusivity ('لا نسلم أنه مخصوص بعلي'); second, they report that all entered into the verse ('بل يروى أنه دخل فيه جميع').
This response directly undermines the proof.
4
Reflect on the significance
Ask yourself: why does this refutation matter? It shows that a verse used to support a specific claim does not actually support it exclusively. This is a common method in theological debate.
Connects to the broader theme of objective interpretation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال أصحابنا: أما التمسك في التفضيل بقوله تعالى: وَأَنفُسَنَا وَأَنفُسَكُم [آل عمران: 61] فالجواب عنه: لا نسلم أنه مخصوص بعلي، بل يروى أنه دخل فيه جميع.
Our companions said: As for using the saying of the Exalted, 'and our souls and your souls' [Al 'Imran 3:61] as a proof for superiority, the answer to it is: we do not concede that it is exclusive to ‘Ali; rather, it is narrated that all (others) entered into it.
Source pages p. 466

Commentary
This passage presents an objection to the proof for Ali's superiority based on verse 3:61 (the mubahala verse), where the Prophet is commanded to call 'our souls and your souls'. Some claim that 'our souls' refers exclusively to Ali. The author's companions respond by denying exclusivity: they do not concede (la nusallim) that the verse is specific to Ali, and they report that the verse includes all the people involved (possibly referring to all those who were present in the mubahala, such as the Prophet's family). This response undermines the argument that the verse singles out Ali. The passage is incomplete in the window, but the initial response is clear.

Key Terms
التمسك (clinging / using as proof)
التفضيل (preference/superiority)
آية المباهلة (verse of mubahala)
مخصوص (exclusive)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some believe that verse 3:61 exclusively refers to Ali, thus proving his superiority over the rest of the Companions.
Correction: The companions respond that the verse is not exclusive to Ali; rather, it includes all the participants (perhaps the Prophet's family or the people involved in the mubahala).

Checkpoint
How do the author's companions respond to the claim that verse 3:61 proves Ali's exclusive superiority?
A) They accept the claim but add another proof
B) They deny exclusivity, saying the verse includes others
C) They argue the verse is abrogated
D) They ignore the claim

Key Takeaway
The claim of exclusive superiority from verse 3:61 is refuted by denying exclusivity and affirming that the verse includes all participants.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read the verse 'and our souls and your souls' (3:61), do you tend to assume it refers to only one person? How does the response in this lesson challenge you to examine your own assumptions when interpreting sacred texts? Will you commit to seeking broader narrations before forming conclusions?
?
How often do you accept claims of exclusivity—that something is only for a specific person or group—without checking if there is evidence that includes others? Can you recall a situation in your own life where you assumed something was exclusive, but later found out it applied to everyone? How will you change this habit?
?
If you were to defend a belief based on a verse, would you be honest enough to consider counter-narrations like this one? What does that say about your sincerity in seeking truth, and will you commit to always seeking all available evidence before asserting a position?

Ali's High Lineage
Ali's lineage as a proof of his superiority.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument for Ali's superiority based on his high lineage, particularly his being a Hashimi from both parents and his close relation to the Prophet.

Core Idea
Ali's lineage was unique: a Hashimi from both sides, closest to the Prophet.

This lesson examines the first argument presented by those who claim Ali ibn Abi Talib is superior to Abu Bakr. The argument focuses on lineage: Ali was a Hashimi from both father and mother, and his grandfathers were the noblest. The author contrasts this with the lineage of al-Abbas and Abu Talib to highlight Ali's distinction.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully to understand the lineage argument.
Note the comparison with al-Abbas and Abu Talib.
2
Identify the key points
The passage makes two points: (a) Ali was closest to the Prophet in lineage, (b) he was a Hashimi from both parents.
3
Reflect on the argument's strength
Consider why lineage was considered a criterion for superiority in early Islam and how this argument fits the broader debate.
This is a sectarian argument, not necessarily the author's own view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المسألة التاسعة والثلاثون
الهام قطع الأقلام ومنها النسب العالي ومعلوم أن أشرف الأنساب هو القرب من رسول الله، وهو كان أقرب الناس في النسب إلى رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم وأما العباس، فإنه وإن كان عم رسول الله إلا أن العباس كان أخا لعبد الله والد رسول الله من الأب لا من الأم، وأما أبو طالب فإنه كان أخا لعبد الله والد رسول الله من الأب والأم.
وأيضًا فإن عليا كان هاشميًا من الأب والأم ؛ لأنه علي بن أبي طالب بن عبد المطلب بن هاشم وأيضًا أم علي بن أبي طالب فاطمة أسد بنت بن هاشم ومنها المصاهرة ولم يكن لأحد من الخلق مصاهرة مثل ما كانت له. وأما عثمان فهو وإن شركه في كونه صهرًا للرسول إلا أن أشرف أولاد الرسول هو فاطمة، ولذلك قال : سيدة نساء العالمين أربع وعدها منهن. ولم يحصل مثل هذا الشرف للبنتين اللتين هما زوجتا عثمان ومنها : أنه لم يكن لأحد من الصحابة أولاد يشاركون أولاده في الفضيلة كالحسن والحسين، وهما سيدا شباب أهل الجنة - ولداه.
ثم انظر إلى أولاد الحسن مثل الحسن - المثنى والمثلث - وعبد الله المثنى، والنفس الزكية وإلى أولاد الحسين مثل زين العابدين، والباقر والصادق، والكاظم، والرضا، فإن هؤلاء الأكابر يقر بفضيلتهم وعلو درجتهم كل مسلم.
ومما يدل على علو شأنهم أن أفضل المشايخ وأعلاهم درجة هو أبو يزيد البسطامي، وكان نشأ في دار جعفر الصادق، وأما معروف الكرخي فإنه أسلم على يد علي بن موسى الرضا، وكان بواب داره وبقي على هذه الحالة إلى آخر عمره، ومعلوم أن أمثال هذه الأولاد لم يتفق لأحد من الصحابة.
ولو أخذنا في الشرح والإطناب لطال الكلام.
فهذا مجموع دلائل من قال بتفضيل أمير المؤمنين علي بن أبي
The thirty-ninth issue. The hint: cutting of pens, and among them is high lineage. It is known that the noblest of lineages is closeness to the Messenger of God, and he was the closest of people in lineage to the Messenger of God, may God bless him and grant him peace. As for al-Abbas, although he was the uncle of the Messenger of God, al-Abbas was the brother of Abdullah, the father of the Messenger of God, from the father but not from the mother. As for Abu Talib, he was the brother of Abdullah, the father of the Messenger of God, from both father and mother. Also, Ali was a Hashimi from both father and mother, because he is Ali ibn Abi Talib ibn Abd al-Muttalib ibn Hashim; also the mother of Ali ibn Abi Talib, Fatima bint Asad, was from the family of Hashim. Among them is marriage alliance, and no one among creation had a marriage alliance like his. As for Uthman, although he shared with him in being a son-in-law of the Messenger, the noblest of the Messenger's children is Fatima, and therefore he said: 'The mistress of the women of the worlds is four' and he counted her among them. Such honor was not attained by the two daughters who were wives of Uthman. Among them is that none of the Companions had children who shared with his children in virtue like al-Hasan and al-Husayn, and they are the masters of the youth of Paradise—his two sons. Then look at the children of al-Hasan like al-Hasan al-Muthanna and al-Muthallath, and Abdullah al-Muthanna, and al-Nafs al-Zakiyya, and at the children of al-Husayn like Zayn al-Abidin, al-Baqir, al-Sadiq, al-Kazim, and al-Rida, for these great ones, every Muslim acknowledges their virtue and high rank. Among what indicates their high standing is that the best of the shaykhs and the highest in degree is Abu Yazid al-Bistami, and he grew up in the house of Ja'far al-Sadiq. As for Ma'ruf al-Karkhi, he converted to Islam at the hands of Ali ibn Musa al-Rida, and he was the doorkeeper of his house and remained in this state until the end of his life. It is known that the like of these children did not occur for any of the Companions. If we were to elaborate and go into detail, the speech would become lengthy. This is the total of the proofs of those who said that the Commander of the Faithful Ali ibn Abi is superior.
Source pages p. 466

Commentary
This passage enumerates the arguments of those who claim Ali ibn Abi Talib is superior to Abu Bakr. It is presented as the opponent's case, to be refuted later. The arguments are: (1) Ali's high lineage—he was the closest in lineage to the Prophet, being a Hashimi from both parents, unlike al-Abbas or Abu Talib. (2) His marriage to Fatima, the noblest of the Prophet's daughters, who was declared the mistress of the women of the worlds. (3) His children, al-Hasan and al-Husayn, and their descendants, who are universally recognized as virtuous, with great saints like Abu Yazid al-Bistami and Ma'ruf al-Karkhi being connected to them. The passage concludes that this is the sum of their proofs.

Key Terms
نسب عالي
هاشمي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why was Ali's lineage superior to that of al-Abbas?
A) Because Ali was a Hashimi from both parents, while al-Abbas was only from the father.
B) Because Ali was the Prophet's cousin.
C) Because al-Abbas was not a Hashimi.
D) Because Ali was the son of Abu Talib.

Key Takeaway
Ali's unique Hashimi lineage from both parents is used as a proof of his superiority over other Companions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider your own lineage and status in this life, how does it compare to the value of being spiritually close to the Prophet? Are you more concerned about your worldly family name or about your connection to the Sunnah? Write one specific action you will take this week to increase your closeness to the Prophet in practice.
?
The passage highlights that Ali's honor came from his lineage to the Prophet. Reflect: What is your 'lineage' to the Prophet? Is it merely by claiming to follow Islam, or are you actively forging a connection through love, obedience, and emulation? Name one aspect of the Prophet's character you will consciously adopt tomorrow.
?
If someone were to judge your 'noble lineage' in the sight of God, what would it be based on? Does your heart yearn for the honor of being near the Prophet in the Hereafter, or are you content with worldly accolades? Set a practical goal to read the Prophet's biography (sira) with the intention of increasing love.

Ali's Marriage Alliance
Ali's marriage to Fatima as a proof of superiority.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ali's marriage to Fatima, the noblest of the Prophet's daughters, is used as an argument for his superiority.

Core Idea
Ali's marriage to Fatima, the best of women, set him apart.

The second argument for Ali's superiority concerns his marriage alliance (musahara). The author argues that no one had a marriage alliance like Ali's: he married Fatima, the noblest of the Prophet's children, whom the Prophet declared the mistress of the women of the worlds. This honor is contrasted with Uthman's marriage to other daughters.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation focusing on the marriage argument.
Notice the specific honor given to Fatima.
2
Compare with Uthman
The author mentions Uthman also being a son-in-law but contrasts the status of Fatima with Uthman's wives.
This highlights the unique honor of Fatima.
3
Reflect on the criterion
Consider why being the husband of the best daughter would imply superiority.

المسألة التاسعة والثلاثون
الهام قطع الأقلام ومنها النسب العالي ومعلوم أن أشرف الأنساب هو القرب من رسول الله، وهو كان أقرب الناس في النسب إلى رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم وأما العباس، فإنه وإن كان عم رسول الله إلا أن العباس كان أخا لعبد الله والد رسول الله من الأب لا من الأم، وأما أبو طالب فإنه كان أخا لعبد الله والد رسول الله من الأب والأم.
وأيضًا فإن عليا كان هاشميًا من الأب والأم ؛ لأنه علي بن أبي طالب بن عبد المطلب بن هاشم وأيضًا أم علي بن أبي طالب فاطمة أسد بنت بن هاشم ومنها المصاهرة ولم يكن لأحد من الخلق مصاهرة مثل ما كانت له. وأما عثمان فهو وإن شركه في كونه صهرًا للرسول إلا أن أشرف أولاد الرسول هو فاطمة، ولذلك قال : سيدة نساء العالمين أربع وعدها منهن. ولم يحصل مثل هذا الشرف للبنتين اللتين هما زوجتا عثمان ومنها : أنه لم يكن لأحد من الصحابة أولاد يشاركون أولاده في الفضيلة كالحسن والحسين، وهما سيدا شباب أهل الجنة - ولداه.

Ali's Progeny
Ali's children and descendants as proof of his superiority.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ali's children and descendants, particularly al-Hasan and al-Husayn and their lines, are used as evidence for his superiority.

Core Idea
Ali's children, the masters of Paradise's youth, and their saintly descendants prove his superiority.

The third argument for Ali's superiority focuses on his progeny. The passage asserts that no other Companion had children as virtuous as al-Hasan and al-Husayn, who are the masters of the youth of Paradise. It then lists their revered descendants, including figures like Zayn al-Abidin and Ja'far al-Sadiq, and notes that great saints like Abu Yazid al-Bistami and Ma'ruf al-Karkhi were connected to them. This lineage of virtue is unique to Ali.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation to understand the argument from progeny.
Pay attention to the long list of descendants.
2
Identify the key figures
Note the specific descendants mentioned: al-Hasan, al-Husayn, their children, and the later Imams.
These figures are widely respected across Muslim traditions.
3
Understand the saintly connections
The passage mentions Abu Yazid al-Bistami and Ma'ruf al-Karkhi to show that even great saints were linked to Ali's descendants.
This underscores their spiritual stature.
4
Reflect on the argument's scope
Consider why the enduring virtue of a person's descendants can be a mark of that person's own merit.

?

?

Take a moment to repent for any negligence in your heart and resolve to increase your love and respect for them.
?

But think: if these proofs are so strong that even the opponents use them to argue for Ali's superiority, what does that mean for your own stance on the virtues of the Companions? Are you willing to accept the virtue of Ali and his progeny without diminishing the virtues of others? Is there any jealousy or bias in your heart that prevents you from acknowledging the excellence of the Ahl al-Bayt? Pray to Allah to purify your heart.

Refutation of the Mubahala Argument for Ali's Preference
Imam Razi refutes using the Mubahala verse exclusively for Ali.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the twofold refutation of the claim that Quran 3:61 (Mubahala) establishes Ali's exclusive superiority over other companions.

Core Idea
The Mubahala verse does not prove Ali's exclusive virtue; it includes others and is counterbalanced.

In theological debates, a key argument for Ali's superiority is the verse of Mubahala (Quran 3:61), where the Prophet included 'our souls' and 'your souls.' Imam Razi offers a clear response: the verse is not exclusive to Ali, and it is balanced by another prophetic statement. Let's examine his reasoning directly from the text.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the two distinct counterarguments presented by Imam Razi.
The word 'معارض' means 'counterbalanced' or 'opposed by an equally strong evidence.'
2
Identify the two refutation points
First, the verse is not specific to Ali (it includes relatives and servants). Second, it is counterbalanced by the Prophet's statement to the Ash'aris.
3
Understand the broader context
This is a standard method in kalam: when an opponent cites a proof, you either deny its exclusivity or show it is outweighed by another proof.
This lesson is part of a series on arguments for the preference of certain companions.
4
Test your understanding with the checkpoint
Answer the MCQ to confirm you grasp the main point of the refutation.
The answer is based directly on the phrase 'لا نسلم أنه مخصوص بعلي'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال أصحابنا : أما التمسك في التفضيل بقوله تعالى: وَأَنفُسَنَا وَأَنفُسَكُم الله [آل عمران: ٦١] فالجواب عنه : لا نسلم أنه مخصوص بعلي، بل يروى أنه دخل فيه جميع أقربائه وخدمه ثم إن التمسك به معارض بقوله للأشعريين: هم مني وأنا منهم.
Our companions said: As for clinging, in the matter of preference, to His statement, the Exalted: 'and ourselves and yourselves' [Aal Imran 61], the answer to it is: We do not concede that it is specific to Ali; rather, it is narrated that all his relatives and servants entered into it. Then, clinging to it is counterbalanced by his statement to the Ash'aris: 'They are from me and I am from them.'
Source pages p. 467

Commentary
This passage is the first objection-and-answer. The opponent argues using Quran 3:61 (the verse of Mubahala) to prove Ali's superiority. The author's response is twofold: (1) The verse is not exclusive to Ali; reports indicate it includes all of his relatives and servants. (2) This verse is counterbalanced by another statement of the Prophet about the Ash'aris, which also indicates closeness. Thus, the argument does not establish exclusive preference for Ali.

Key Terms
التمسك
مخصوص
معارض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mubahala verse proves that Ali is the most virtuous companion because the Prophet called him 'our souls.'
Correction: Imam Razi argues the verse is not exclusive to Ali, and it is counterbalanced by other prophetic statements.

Checkpoint
What is the first response to the argument from Quran 3:61?
A) The verse is abrogated
B) The verse is not specific to Ali
C) The verse is about Abu Bakr
D) The verse is unclear

Key Takeaway
Imam Razi refutes the claim of Ali's exclusive superiority from Quran 3:61 by arguing the verse is not exclusive and is counterbalanced by another hadith.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter an argument that seems to exalt one person over others, do you automatically embrace it if it aligns with your loyalties? How would you apply Imam Razi's method of checking exclusivity and counterbalance to your own beliefs about people you admire?
?
This lesson shows that even a Quranic verse can be contextualized and counterbalanced by other evidence. Are you willing to let go of a cherished interpretation if stronger evidence challenges it? What would that cost you?
?
The Prophet said to the Ash'aris: 'They are from me and I am from them,' demonstrating that closeness to the Prophet is not limited to a single person. Do you measure your own closeness to the Prophet by exclusive claims of a group, or by your own actions and adherence to his sunnah?

Refutation of the Hadith of the Bird Argument
Imam Razi critiques the ambiguity of the term 'most beloved' in the hadith.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Razi critiques the Hadith of the Bird by exposing the ambiguity of the phrase 'most beloved' and why this refutation is considered strong.

Core Idea
Ambiguity cannot prove absolute superiority.

Imam Razi continues refuting arguments for the superiority of one companion over another. Here he addresses the famous Hadith of the Bird, which is often cited as proof. Rather than reject the hadith outright, he shows that its language is ambiguous and does not necessitate general preference. This is a subtle but powerful critique.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic original and its literal English translation. Note the key terms and the flow of the argument.
Pay attention to how Razi introduces the objection.
2
Identify the two possible meanings
Razi states that 'most beloved of Your creation' could mean either in all matters or in a specific matter. Identify these two interpretations in the text.
The phrase 'يحتمل' signals the ambiguity.
3
Understand the evidence for ambiguity
Razi argues that the phrase can be divided into two possibilities, proving it is not decisive. Understand why division shows ambiguity.
He uses logical division (تقسيم) to show the expression is ambiguous.
4
Trace the conclusion
Follow how Razi concludes that the expression only indicates superiority in some matters, not all, and thus does not prove absolute preference.
The conclusion 'لا يوجب التفضيل' is key.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الثاني: وهو التمسك بخبر الطير فالاعتراض عليه أن نقول : قوله : بأحب خلقك يحتمل أن يكون أحب خلق الله في جميع الأمور، أو يكون أحب خلق الله في شيء معين، والدليل على كونه محتملا لهما أنه يصح تقسيمه إليهما، فيقال : إما أن يكون أحب خلقه إليه في كل الأمور أو يكون أحب خلقه إليه في هذا الأمر الواحد، وما به الاشتراك غير ما به الاختلاف وغير مستلزم له، فإذن هذا اللفظ لا يدل على كونه أحب إلى الله تعالى في جميع الأمور، فإذن هذا اللفظ لا يدل إلا على أنه أحب إليه في بعض الأمور، وهذا يفيد كونه أزيد ثوابا من غيره في بعض الأمور، ولا يمتنع كون غيره أزيد ثوابًا منه في أمر آخر، فثبت أن هذا لا يوجب التفضيل، وهذا جواب قوي.
As for the second: that is clinging to the hadith of the bird. The objection against it is that we say: His statement 'with the most beloved of Your creation' is likely to mean the most beloved of God's creation in all matters, or the most beloved of God's creation in a specific thing. The evidence that it is capable of both meanings is that it is valid to divide it into two, so it is said: Either he is the most beloved of His creation to Him in all matters, or he is the most beloved of His creation to Him in this one matter. That by which they share is not that by which they differ, and it does not necessitate it. So then this expression does not indicate that he is the most beloved to God Almighty in all matters. So then this expression only indicates that he is the most beloved to Him in some matters, and this entails that he is greater in reward than others in some matters, and it is not impossible that another is greater in reward than him in another matter. So it is established that this does not necessitate preference, and this is a strong answer.
Source pages p. 467

Commentary
This passage is the second objection-and-answer, concerning the hadith of the bird (where the Prophet prayed for the 'most beloved of creation' to eat with him, and Ali came). The author's refutation hinges on ambiguity: the phrase 'most beloved of Your creation' could mean beloved in all matters or only in a specific matter. Since it is ambiguous, it cannot prove absolute superiority. The word may only indicate superiority in that particular situation, and another person could be superior in other ways. Therefore, this hadith does not obligate general preference.

Key Terms

أحب خلقك
احتمال
تقسيم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception: The Hadith of the Bird definitively proves Ali's absolute superiority over all companions.
Correction: The author shows that the phrase 'most beloved' is ambiguous and could refer to a specific context, so it does not prove overall superiority.

Checkpoint
Why does the author consider the hadith of the bird insufficient to prove Ali's absolute superiority?
A) The hadith is weak in transmission
B) The phrase 'most beloved' is ambiguous and could be specific
C) The hadith contradicts the Quran

The Hadith of the Bird does not prove absolute superiority because the phrase 'most beloved' is ambiguous and could refer to a specific context.

Refutation of the Knowledge Argument
Imam Razi argues that Ali's knowledge may have been acquired after Abu Bakr's lifetime.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Razi refutes the claim that Ali's superiority is proven by his knowledge.

Core Idea
Knowledge must be compared at the same time to decide preference.

Imam Razi addresses a common argument for Abu Bakr's superiority: that Ali was more knowledgeable. He provides a sharp chronological refutation, showing that knowledge acquired later does not prove superiority at an earlier time. This passage teaches us the importance of timing in comparative judgments.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once and then the translation. Notice the structure: opponent's claim followed by Razi's response.
2
Identify the opponent's claim
The opponent argues that Ali was more knowledgeable ('aalam). Locate this in the passage.
The phrase 'كان أعلم' is key.
3
Understand Razi's response
Razi says it is possible Ali gained his knowledge after Abu Bakr's death. Why does this refute the opponent?
The response hinges on the phrase 'عاش بعده زمانا طويلا'.
4
Reflect on the principle
This argument teaches that when comparing two people, we must consider the timing of their qualities. Knowledge at death does not necessarily apply to an earlier period.
This is a general logical principle in kalam debates.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما الحجة الثالثة : وهي أن عليا كان أعلم. قلنا : لم لا يجوز أن يقال: إنه حصل له هذه العلوم الكثيرة بعد أبي بكر، وذلك لأنه عاش بعده زمانا طويلا، فلعله حصلها في هذه المدة، فلم قلتم إنه في زمان حياة أبي بكر كان أعلم منه.
As for the third proof: that Ali was more knowledgeable. We say: Why is it not permissible to say that he acquired these many sciences after Abu Bakr, and that because he lived after him for a long time, perhaps he acquired them in this period? So why do you say that during the lifetime of Abu Bakr he was more knowledgeable than him?
Source pages p. 467

Commentary
This passage is the third objection-and-answer. The opponent argues that Ali was more knowledgeable ('aalam). The author's response is based on chronology: Ali outlived Abu Bakr by many years. It is possible that Ali acquired his extensive knowledge after Abu Bakr's death. Therefore, there is no evidence that Ali was more knowledgeable during Abu Bakr's lifetime, which is the relevant comparison for preference.

Key Terms
أعلم
حصل
عاش بعده

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Assuming that a person's total knowledge at any point in life can be used to compare status at an earlier time.
Correction: The author argues that knowledge may be acquired later, so it does not apply to the earlier period under comparison.

Checkpoint
What is the author's main point in refuting the argument that Ali was more knowledgeable?
A) Ali never claimed to be knowledgeable
B) Knowledge is not a criterion for preference
C) Ali's knowledge was acquired after Abu Bakr's death
D) Abu Bakr was also very knowledgeable

Key Takeaway
The timing of knowledge acquisition is crucial when comparing figures; later knowledge does not retroactively prove earlier superiority.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look up to someone for guidance, do you evaluate their knowledge based on what they knew at the time they advised you, or on everything they later learned? How might your judgment be unfair if you do the latter?
?

?
Are there people you dismiss as less knowledgeable now, but who may have been more knowledgeable at a crucial moment in your life? How does this lesson challenge you to reassess their role?

Refuting the Claim: Ali's Jihad vs Abu Bakr's Da'wa
Imam Razi distinguishes types of jihad and argues Abu Bakr's da'wa was more impactful.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Razi categorizes jihad and why he argues that Abu Bakr's da'wa (calling to Islam) was more impactful than Ali's military jihad.

Core Idea
Abu Bakr's da'wa converted major Companions, strengthening Islam more than any battle.

This passage presents Imam Razi's response to those who claim Ali's jihad was greater than Abu Bakr's. Razi introduces a threefold categorization of jihad: against the self, with evidence, and with the sword. He then argues that Abu Bakr excelled in the jihad of da'wa, as his efforts led to the conversion of key Companions, greatly empowering the early Muslim community.

Guided Path
1
Understand the context
The opponent claims that Ali's jihad was greater than Abu Bakr's. Read the opening lines to see how Razi frames his response.
2
Learn the three types of jihad
Razi divides jihad into three categories: against the self, against the enemy with evidence, and against the enemy with the sword. Identify each one in the passage.
This categorization is key to Razi's argument.
3
Analyze the superiority of Abu Bakr's da'wa
Razi provides two proofs that Abu Bakr's da'wa was superior. The first proof lists the prominent Companions Abu Bakr brought to Islam. Note how their conversion strengthened the Muslim community.
Contrast with Ali, whose conversion did not immediately attract others.
4
Reflect on the significance
Consider why Razi considers da'wa a form of jihad and how this reframes the debate about the merits of early Companions.
This passage highlights the importance of da'wa in early Islam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الحجة الرابعة وهي قوله : 'إن جهاد علي كان أكثر'. قلنا : الجهاد أقسام منها جهاد مع النفس ومنها جهاد مع العدو بالحجة، ومنها جهاد العدو بالسيف والسنان، أما الجهاد مع النفس قلا نسلم أن عليا كان أقوى فيه من أبي بكر وأما الجهاد مع العدو بالحجة والدعوة إلى الله فكان أمر أبي بكر فيه أتم، ويدل عليه وجهان : الأول : أنه لما أسلم أبو بكر اشتغل في تلك الأيام بالدعوة إلى الله تعالى، فدعا إلى الله عثمان بن عفان وطلحة والزبير وسعد بن أبي وقاص وعثمان بن مظعهون، وهؤلاء هم أكابر الصحابة وجاء بهم إلى رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم حتى أسلموا على يده وحصل بسبب ذلك للإسلام قوة عظيمة، فحصلت بسبب إسلام أبي بكر هذه القوة العظيمة، أما علي فحين أسلم لم يصر إسلامه سببا لإسلام غيره، ومن المعلوم أن هذه الطاعة مما لا يوازيها شيء من الطاعات.
As for the fourth proof: his statement 'Indeed, the jihad of Ali was greater.' We say: Jihad has divisions: among them is jihad against the self, among them is jihad against the enemy with evidence, and among them is jihad against the enemy with sword and spear. As for jihad against the self, we do not concede that Ali was stronger in it than Abu Bakr. As for jihad against the enemy with evidence and calling to God, the matter of Abu Bakr was more complete in it. There are two proofs: First, when Abu Bakr accepted Islam, he occupied himself in those days with calling to God Almighty, so he called to God Uthman ibn Affan, Talhah, al-Zubayr, Sa'd ibn Abi Waqqas, and Uthman ibn Maz'un, and these are the major Companions, and he brought them to the Messenger of God, may God bless him and grant him peace, until they accepted Islam at his hands. Because of that, a great strength occurred for Islam. So this great strength occurred due to Abu Bakr's Islam. As for Ali, when he accepted Islam, his Islam did not become a cause for the Islam of others. It is known that this act is not paralleled by any acts of obedience.
Source pages p. 467

Commentary
This passage is the fourth objection-and-answer. The opponent claims Ali's jihad was greater. The author refutes by categorizing jihad into three types: against the self, against the enemy with evidence (da'wa), and with sword. For jihad against the self, Ali is not conceded to be superior. For jihad with evidence, Abu Bakr excelled because his conversion led to the conversion of major Companions like Uthman, Talhah, al-Zubayr, Sa'd, and Uthman ibn Maz'un, greatly strengthening Islam. Ali's conversion did not immediately cause others to convert. This act of da'wa is unmatched.

Key Terms
جهاد
جهاد النفس
جهاد الحجة
الدعوة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Jihad is only military fighting.
Correction: Imam Razi expands jihad to include spiritual struggle and da'wa (calling to Islam), showing that Abu Bakr's da'wa was a superior form of jihad.

Checkpoint
According to Imam Razi, why was Abu Bakr's jihad more impactful than Ali's?
A) He fought more battles
B) He was wealthier
C) His da'wa led to the conversion of major Companions
D) He was older

Key Takeaway
Imam Razi argues that Abu Bakr's da'wa, which led to the conversion of major Companions, was a greater jihad than Ali's military efforts.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you became more conscious of your faith, did you actively seek to invite others to truth as Abu Bakr did, or did you keep it private? What excuses are you making to avoid da'wa?
?
Which form of jihad do you prioritize in your life: internal spiritual struggle, intellectual advocacy, or physical effort? How does that align with the greater good of the Muslim community?
?
If your Islam has never caused anyone else to consider faith, what might that say about the sincerity and strength of your own belief? What will you change starting today?

Razi's Refutation of the Bird Story Argument
Razi refutes the bird story argument by analyzing ambiguity in the Prophet's words.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Razi uses linguistic ambiguity to refute the claim that the hadith of the bird proves Ali's absolute superiority over others.

Core Idea
Ambiguous words cannot prove absolute preference.

Imam Razi now turns to the second argument for Ali's superiority, based on the famous 'bird story' hadith. Rather than rejecting the tradition outright, he examines the key phrase 'the most beloved of Your creation' and shows that it is ambiguous. This lesson walks through his careful linguistic analysis and why it undermines the claim of absolute preference.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its literal translation to grasp Razi's argument.
Notice how Razi starts by stating the objection.
2
Identify the two possible meanings
Razi says the phrase 'the most beloved of Your creation' could mean overall love or specific love. Find where he explains this.
He uses the logical division: either overall or specific.
3
Understand why ambiguity defeats the argument
Ask yourself why the possibility of a specific meaning prevents the phrase from proving absolute superiority. Razi's conclusion follows from this.
The key point: commonality does not entail specificity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الثاني: وهو التمسك بخبر الطير فالاعتراض عليه أن نقول : قوله : بأحب خلقك يحتمل أن يكون أحب خلق الله في جميع الأمور، أو يكون أحب خلق الله في شيء معين، والدليل على كونه محتملا لهما أنه يصح تقسيمه إليهما، فيقال : إما أن يكون أحب خلقه إليه في كل الأمور أو يكون أحب خلقه إليه في هذا الأمر الواحد، وما به الاشتراك غير ما به الاختلاف وغير مستلزم له، فإذن هذا اللفظ لا يدل على كونه أحب إلى الله تعالى في جميع الأمور، فإذن هذا اللفظ لا يدل إلا على أنه أحب إليه في بعض الأمور، وهذا يفيد كونه أزيد ثوابا من غيره في بعض الأمور، ولا يمتنع كون غيره أزيد ثوابًا منه في أمر آخر، فثبت أن هذا لا يوجب التفضيل، وهذا جواب قوي.
As for the second [argument], which is the adherence to the tradition of the bird, the objection to it is to say: his statement: 'by the most beloved of Your creation' could mean the most beloved of God's creation in all matters, or it could mean the most beloved of God's creation in a specific matter. The proof that it can bear both meanings is that it is valid to divide it into these two possibilities. So it is said: either he is the most beloved of His creation to Him in all matters, or he is the most beloved of His creation to Him in this one matter. And that which is common [between the two meanings] is not that which is different, and it does not entail it. Therefore, this expression does not indicate that he is the most beloved to God Almighty in all matters. Therefore, this expression only indicates that he is the most beloved to Him in some matters. And this implies that he has more reward than others in some matters, and it is not impossible that someone else has more reward than him in another matter. So it is established that this [argument] does not necessitate preference [over others], and this is a strong response.
Source pages p. 467

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the second argument for Ali's superiority, which relies on the hadith of the bird. Razi's method is to analyze the phrase 'the most beloved of Your creation' for ambiguity. He argues that the phrase could mean either overall or specific love. Since the phrase can be divided into two possible meanings, the common meaning (love) is not the same as the specific difference (whether overall or specific), and the one does not entail the other. Thus the phrase does not decisively indicate overall love. At best it indicates love in some matters, which only means Ali has more reward in some matters, not overall. So the argument fails to prove superiority. Razi calls this a strong response.

Key Terms
خبر الطير
أحب خلقك
تفضيل
احتمال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any expression of love from the Prophet implies absolute superiority.
Correction: Razi shows that such expressions can be limited to specific contexts and do not necessarily indicate overall preference.

Checkpoint
According to Razi's refutation of the bird story argument, why does the phrase 'the most beloved of Your creation' not prove Ali's overall superiority?
A) Because the hadith is weak.
B) Because the phrase is ambiguous and could mean love in a specific matter rather than all matters.
C) Because the phrase was said to Abu Bakr, not Ali.
D) Because the Prophet did not intend to give any preference.

Key Takeaway
Razi refutes the bird story argument by showing that the Prophet's words are ambiguous, and ambiguous words cannot be used to prove absolute superiority.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read this passage, are you willing to admit that some of your cherished beliefs might be based on ambiguous evidence? Have you ever held onto a view because of an expression that could mean something different? What would it take for you to re-examine that belief today?
?
Razi shows that even a saying of the Prophet can be interpreted in two ways. How often do you accept a religious argument without considering its possible ambiguities? Will you commit to checking the evidence more carefully from now on?
?
If you were to apply Razi's logical approach to a personal disagreement with a fellow Muslim, would you be as ready to accept that your interpretation might be just one possibility? What is one concrete step you can take to listen more carefully to the other side?

Refuting the Claim of Superior Knowledge by Chronology
Razi refutes the argument that Ali was more knowledgeable by invoking chronology.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi uses the chronological factor to refute the argument that Ali's greater knowledge proves his superiority over Abu Bakr.

Core Idea
Chronology matters: knowledge gained later does not prove superiority during an earlier period.

Among the arguments for Ali's superiority was his greater knowledge. Razi counters this by pointing out that Ali lived much longer after Abu Bakr, so his knowledge could have been acquired later. This lesson examines that refutation and the logical principle behind it.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Notice how Razi directly engages the claim without dismissing it outright.
The passage is short but packed with logical reasoning.
2
Identify the claim and refutation
Pinpoint the claim ('Ali was more knowledgeable') and Razi's refutation: the knowledge could have been acquired after Abu Bakr's death. Ask yourself what assumption the claim makes about timing.
The refutation does not deny Ali's knowledge, only its relevance to the period in question.
3
Understand the logical structure
Map the argument: if knowledge can be acquired later, then having more knowledge at a later time does not imply having more knowledge at an earlier time. This undermines the claim of superiority during Abu Bakr's life.
This is a classic example of chronological fallacy.
4
Apply the principle
Consider how this reasoning applies in other debates: e.g., a student who becomes an expert later in life was not necessarily more knowledgeable than their teacher at the time of study.
The key is to evaluate claims in their proper temporal context.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما الحجة الثالثة : وهي أن عليا كان أعلم. قلنا : لم لا يجوز أن يقال: إنه حصل له هذه العلوم الكثيرة بعد أبي بكر، وذلك لأنه عاش بعده زمانا طويلا، فلعله حصلها في هذه المدة، فلم قلتم إنه في زمان حياة أبي بكر كان أعلم منه.
As for the third argument: that Ali was more knowledgeable. We say: why is it not permissible to say that he acquired these many sciences after Abu Bakr, because he lived after him for a long time, so perhaps he acquired them in that period. So why do you claim that during Abu Bakr's lifetime he was more knowledgeable than him?
Source pages p. 467

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the third argument, which asserts that Ali was more knowledgeable than Abu Bakr. Razi's response is to point out the chronological factor: Ali lived much longer after Abu Bakr's death, so the knowledge Ali possessed later in life does not prove he was more knowledgeable during Abu Bakr's lifetime. The argument does not establish superiority at the time of the Prophet's death or during the early caliphate.

Key Terms
أعلم
عاش بعده زمانا طويلا

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If someone is ultimately more knowledgeable, they must have been more knowledgeable at all earlier times.
Correction: Knowledge can be acquired later, so greater knowledge later does not imply greater knowledge earlier. The relevant time period must be considered.

Checkpoint
How does Razi refute the claim that Ali was more knowledgeable than Abu Bakr?
A) He denies that Ali had any knowledge.
B) He argues that Ali's greater knowledge came after Abu Bakr's death, so it does not prove superiority during Abu Bakr's life.
C) He claims Abu Bakr was actually more knowledgeable.
D) He says knowledge is irrelevant for superiority.

Key Takeaway
Razi refutes the argument for Ali's superiority based on knowledge by showing that later acquisition of knowledge does not prove superiority at an earlier time.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you learn something new today, do you ever look back and assume you always knew it? How does Razi's argument expose the error of projecting your current knowledge onto your past? Pause and think of one specific instance where you misjudged someone's past ability based on later achievements. Will you repent from that unfair judgment today?
?
You likely evaluate your own knowledge growth: are you more knowledgeable now than a year ago? If so, would it be fair for someone to say you were always at that level? How does this lesson renew your intention to be humble about what you have learned recently, rather than using it to claim superiority over others?
?
In debates about who is ‘better’ among the Companions, people often cherry-pick later events. Razi demands evidence for the earlier period. In your own life, are you quick to assume others' past deficiencies based on one later success or failure? What would it take for you to only judge others based on what was true at the time? Ask Allah to grant you fairness and precision in your assessments.

Refutation of the Claim that Ali's Jihad was Greater
Razi refutes the argument that Ali's jihad was greater by classifying jihad into types and providing two proofs favoring Abu Bakr.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi refutes the argument that Ali's jihad was greater by distinguishing three types of jihad and highlighting Abu Bakr's superiority in jihad with argument and da'wah.

Core Idea
Jihad is more than fighting; it includes calling to Allah and defending the faith, where Abu Bakr excelled.

Razi addresses a sectarian claim that Ali's physical jihad was superior. But he shows that jihad has multiple dimensions. In this passage, he classifies jihad into three categories and provides two proofs demonstrating that Abu Bakr's efforts in da'wah and early support for the Prophet were unmatched. This reframes the discussion, emphasizing that true greatness lies in the comprehensive struggle for Allah's cause.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading Razi's Arabic argument and its English translation. Notice the structure: he first classifies jihad, then gives two proofs.
Pay attention to how he refutes the claim without denying Ali's merits.
2
Identify the three types of jihad
Razi lists three divisions: jihad against the self, against the enemy with argument, and with sword. Which one is the focus of his refutation?
He concedes nothing on the first, focuses on the second.
3
Examine the first proof: Abu Bakr's da'wah
Razi argues that Abu Bakr's conversion led to the conversion of key Companions, bringing great strength to Islam. Ali's conversion did not have such an effect.
This is a quantitative and qualitative argument.
4
Examine the second proof: Abu Bakr's early defense
Abu Bakr was constantly defending the Prophet during the Meccan and early Medinan periods, while Ali was young and only engaged in later physical fighting.
Thus, Abu Bakr's jihad started earlier and was more sustained.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
Razi concludes that the beginning of jihad was for Abu Bakr and its end for Ali. What does this imply about the comparison?
It shifts the focus from quantity to priority and comprehensiveness.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الحجة الرابعة وهي قوله : "إن جهاد علي كان أكثر". قلنا : الجهاد أقسام منها جهاد مع النفس ومنها جهاد مع العدو بالحجة، ومنها جهاد العدو بالسيف والسنان، أما الجهاد مع النفس قلا نسلم أن عليا كان أقوى فيه من أبي بكر وأما الجهاد مع العدو بالحجة والدعوة إلى الله فكان أمر أبي بكر فيه أتم، ويدل عليه وجهان : الأول : أنه لما أسلم أبو بكر اشتغل في تلك الأيام بالدعوة إلى الله تعالى، فدعا إلى الله عثمان بن عفان وطلحة والزبير وسعد بن أبي وقاص وعثمان بن مظعهون، وهؤلاء هم أكابر الصحابة وجاء بهم إلى رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم حتى أسلموا على يده وحصل بسبب ذلك للإسلام قوة عظيمة، فحصلت بسبب إسلام أبي بكر هذه القوة العظيمة، أما علي فحين أسلم لم يصر إسلامه سببا لإسلام غيره، ومن المعلوم أن هذه الطاعة مما لا يوازيها شيء من الطاعات. الثاني : أن أبا بكر لما أسلم كان أبدا في منازعة مع الكفار والمنافرة معهم، وبقي رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم ثلاث عشرة سنة في مكة ثم انتقل إلى المدينة، وبقي هناك سنة أخرى ثم نزلت آية القتال وأبو بكر في تلك المدة الطويلة في مكة والمدينة كان في الذب عن دين الله ومتابعة رسول الله الله وأما علي فإنه في ذلك الوقت كان صغير السن وما كان يخالط القوم، ثم إن بعد نزول آية القتال اشتغل علي [هما؟] بقتال الكفار. يفيد أن فثبت أن ابتداء الجهاد كان لأبي بكر، وآخره لعلي
As for the fourth argument: his statement: 'Indeed Ali's jihad was greater.' We say: Jihad has divisions: from them is jihad against the self, from them is jihad against the enemy with argument, and from them is jihad against the enemy with sword and spear. As for jihad against the self, we do not concede that Ali was stronger in it than Abu Bakr. As for jihad against the enemy with argument and calling to God, the matter of Abu Bakr in it was more complete. Two proofs indicate this: First, that when Abu Bakr embraced Islam, he occupied himself in those days with calling to God Almighty. He called to God Uthman ibn Affan, Talha, Zubayr, Sa'd ibn Abi Waqqas, and Uthman ibn Maz'un. These were the great ones of the Companions, and he brought them to the Messenger of God (peace be upon him) until they embraced Islam at his hand, and due to that a great strength accrued to Islam. Thus, due to Abu Bakr's Islam, this great strength accrued. As for Ali, when he embraced Islam, his Islam did not become a cause for the Islam of others. And it is known that this act of obedience is such that no other act of obedience can equal it. Second, that when Abu Bakr embraced Islam, he was always in conflict with the disbelievers and opposing them, while the Messenger of God (peace be upon him) remained for thirteen years in Mecca, then moved to Medina, and remained there another year, then the verse of fighting was revealed. Abu Bakr in that long period in Mecca and Medina was defending the religion of God and following the Messenger of God. As for Ali, at that time he was young and did not mix with people. Then after the revelation of the verse of fighting, Ali occupied himself [only?] with fighting the disbelievers. It indicates that so it is established that the beginning of jihad was for Abu Bakr, and its end for Ali.
Source pages p. 467

Commentary
This passage refutes the fourth argument that Ali's jihad was greater. Razi first divides jihad into three types: against the self, against the enemy with argument, and against the enemy with weapons. He argues that Abu Bakr was superior in the second type, and provides two proofs. First, Abu Bakr's conversion led to the conversion of many leading Companions, giving Islam great strength—an achievement unmatched by Ali's conversion. Second, Abu Bakr was constantly defending and supporting the Prophet during the long Meccan and early Medinan periods, whereas Ali was young and only engaged in physical fighting later. Thus, Abu Bakr's jihad started earlier and was more comprehensive.

Misconception: Only physical fighting counts as jihad.
Correction: Razi shows that jihad includes struggling against the self, calling to Allah with argument, and defending the faith. Abu Bakr's da'wah and early defense were forms of jihad that were more significant.

Checkpoint
According to Razi, in what type of jihad was Abu Bakr superior to Ali?

Key Takeaway
Razi refutes the claim that Ali's jihad was greater by demonstrating that jihad encompasses more than physical combat, and that Abu Bakr's da'wah and early defense were superior forms of jihad.

?
Razi argues that Abu Bakr's foundational contributions outweigh Ali's later physical struggles. How does this affect your understanding of what Allah values most? Are you tempted to value dramatic, visible actions over patient, consistent service? Examine your heart: which do you truly esteem?

Refuting the Claim of Ali's Earlier Faith
Razi refutes the argument that Ali's earlier faith proves his superiority.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi refutes the argument that Ali's earlier faith proves his superiority, by pointing to contradictory narrations and emphasizing the transformative impact of Abu Bakr's Islam.

Core Idea
Earlier faith does not equal greater impact; Abu Bakr's conversion transformed Islam.

In this passage, Razi addresses a sectarian argument about the timing of faith. He uses two key moves: first, he points out that the historical reports are conflicting; second, he shifts the criterion from chronology to consequence. This lesson unpacks that reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp Razi's argument.
Notice the two distinct refutation points.
2
Identify the two refutation strategies
Razi uses two arguments: contradictory reports and the greater impact of Abu Bakr's Islam.
These are separate moves.
3
Analyze the impact argument
Consider why impact matters more than chronology in this context.
This shifts the criterion from timing to effect.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الحجة الخامسة : وهي قولهم : إن إيمان علي سابق على إيمان أبي بكر. قلنا : وقد ذكرنا أن الأخبار فيه متعارضة وأخبارنا وإن كانت فكذا أخباركم وأيضًا فقد بينا أن إسلام أبي بكر أثر في قوة الإسلام أثرًا عظيما، وما كان إسلام غيره كذلك.
As for the fifth argument: their statement that Ali's faith preceded Abu Bakr's faith. We say: we have already mentioned that the reports concerning it are contradictory. Our reports [are so], and likewise your reports [are so]. Also, we have already shown that Abu Bakr's Islam had a great impact on the strength of Islam, and the Islam of no other was like that.
Source pages p. 467

Commentary
This passage refutes the fifth argument that Ali's earlier faith proves superiority. Razi responds by noting that the narrations about who embraced Islam first are conflicting and inconclusive. Moreover, even if Ali were earlier, the decisive factor is the impact of one's faith: Abu Bakr's Islam had a transformative effect on the Muslim community, which cannot be said of Ali's. Thus, earlier faith alone does not establish overall superiority.

Key Terms
إيمان سابق
أخبار متعارضة
أثر إسلام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Ali's earlier faith proves he is superior to Abu Bakr.
Correction: Razi shows that the reports are conflicting and that the real measure is the positive impact of one's faith.

Checkpoint

A) He admits it but argues it doesn't matter.
B) He says the reports are contradictory and that Abu Bakr's Islam had a greater impact.
C) He denies that Ali was earlier.
D) He says Abu Bakr's faith was actually earlier.

Key Takeaway
Razi refutes the argument for Ali's superiority by highlighting contradictory narrations and emphasizing the transformative impact of Abu Bakr's conversion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever assumed that someone who 'came first' or was 'earlier' in a good deed is automatically better than someone who came later? Examine your own heart: do you judge people by their timeline or by the depth of their impact?
?
If Abu Bakr's conversion had a transformative impact on the community, what about your own faith? Does your Islam strengthen those around you, or is it merely personal? What evidence can you point to?
?
When you hear contradictory reports about religious history, do you cling to the one that supports your preference, or do you honestly acknowledge the ambiguity as Razi does? This lesson demands intellectual honesty. Where in your life are you refusing to admit that evidence is mixed?

Refutation of the Khaybar Story Argument
Razi refutes the Khaybar hadith argument by analyzing the composite description.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Razi refutes the argument from the Khaybar hadith by analyzing the composite nature of the praise and demonstrating that lacking a specific attribute does not imply overall deficiency in virtue.

Core Idea
A single missing quality does not negate overall virtue.

Imam Razi now tackles the sixth argument of his opponents, which relies on the famous hadith of the banner at Khaybar. The opponents claim that this hadith establishes the superiority of Ali over Abu Bakr and Umar. Razi's response is a masterclass in critical thinking: he dissects the hadith's wording, showing that the praise refers to a combination of attributes, and that the absence of one attribute (being 'karrār ghayr farrār') in the other companions does not diminish their overall virtue. He illustrates his point with a striking analogy from Shia theology itself.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
Carefully read the Arabic passage and its English translation. Understand the opponent's argument and Razi's response. Notice the flow from the opponent's claim to Razi's analysis.
The passage is dense; read slowly and refer to the key terms.
2
Identify the key terms and their significance
Focus on 'قصة خيبر', 'كرار غير فرار', and 'تفضيل'. Understand how 'كرار غير فرار' (attacking and not fleeing) is a specific attribute mentioned in the hadith.
3
Analyze Razi's logical move
Razi argues that the hadith describes a composite attribute that was not fully present in Abu Bakr and Umar. Explain why lacking one component of that composite attribute does not entail a deficiency in overall virtue.
This is the core of the refutation.
4
Reflect on the analogy
Consider why Razi uses the example of prophets and angels. How does this analogy strengthen his argument? According to Shia belief, prophets are superior to angels despite the angels' greater physical power.
The analogy shows that superiority is not determined by a single attribute.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الحجة السادسة : وهي التمسك بقصة خيبر - فجوابها : أن ذلك الكلام مجموع الصفات المذكورة في مدح الثاني غير حاصلة في مدح الأول، فلما قال : لأعطين الراية رجلًا يحب الله ورسوله ويحبه الله ورسوله، کرارًا غیر فرار فهذا يدل على أن هذا المجموع ما كان حاصلا لأبي بكر وعمر ؛ لأن كونه غير فرار ما كان حاصلا فيهما، وكان ذلك المجموع غير حاصل فيهما، وعدم كونه كرارًا غير فرار لا في الفضيلة نقصانا يوجب، ألا ترى أن الأنبياء أفضل من الملائكة عند الشيعة، مع أنا نقطع أنه ليس للأنبياء من القدرة الحسية ذرة من القدرة التي للملائكة.
As for the sixth argument: it is the adherence to the story of Khaybar. Its answer is that that statement [in the hadith] is a combination of attributes mentioned in praise of the second [i.e., Ali?] that are not found in the praise of the first [i.e., the one not given the banner]. When he said: 'I will give the banner to a man who loves God and His Messenger, and God and His Messenger love him, one who attacks and does not flee,' this indicates that this combination was not present in Abu Bakr and Umar, because the characteristic of 'not fleeing' was not present in them, so the combination was not present in them. But the lack of being 'attacking and not fleeing' does not necessitate a deficiency in virtue. Do you not see that the prophets are superior to the angels according to the Shia, even though we are certain that the prophets do not have even an atom of the physical power that the angels have?
Source pages p. 467

Commentary
This passage refutes the sixth argument, which uses the hadith of Khaybar where the Prophet says he will give the banner to one who loves God and is bold in battle. Razi points out that the hadith describes a combination of qualities that were not found in Abu Bakr and Umar (since they were not described as 'attacking and not fleeing' in that context). However, lacking one specific quality does not imply an overall deficiency in virtue. He gives an analogy: according to the Shia, prophets are superior to angels even though angels have greater physical power. Thus, not having the specific quality of being 'karrār ghayr farrār' does not diminish the overall virtue of Abu Bakr and Umar.

Key Terms
قصة خيبر
كرار غير فرار
تفضيل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Khaybar hadith proves that Ali is absolutely superior to all other companions because he possesses the praised attribute of 'karrār ghayr farrār'.
Correction: The hadith describes a composite attribute that was not fully present in the other companions, but lacking this specific attribute does not imply overall deficiency in virtue.

Checkpoint
In the refutation of the Khaybar story, what analogy does Razi use to show that lacking a quality does not imply deficiency in virtue?
A) The difference between angels and prophets in terms of power.
B) The difference between scholars and warriors.
C) The difference between rich and poor.
D) The difference between early and late Muslims.

Key Takeaway
Lacking a specific attribute does not diminish overall virtue; superiority is determined by a holistic evaluation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at someone you admire, do you focus on one missing quality and let it undermine their entire worth in your eyes? Whom have you judged harshly based on a single flaw, and how can you apply Razi's lesson to restore a holistic view?
?
Razi uses the example of prophets being superior to angels despite lacking physical power. In your own life, which weakness do you consider disqualifying for being 'good enough' in Allah's sight? What would it mean to believe that Allah sees your whole self, not just that one lack?
?
Think of a time you felt inferior because you didn't measure up to a 'composite' standard—maybe in faith, character, or achievement. How does Razi's argument free you from that comparison? What attribute of yours might be overlooked by others but is valued by Allah?

Razi's Conclusion: Weak Reports and Chapter Closure
Razi dismisses remaining weak reports and concludes the chapter.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi dismisses weak arguments and concludes a theological chapter using a standard scholarly formula.

Core Idea
Razi closes the chapter by dismissing weak reports and mirroring them on both sides.

This short concluding passage marks the end of a lengthy discussion on the merits of Ali and Abu Bakr. Razi dismisses the remaining arguments as weak reports and notes that similar reports exist for Abu Bakr. This demonstrates a fair-minded approach and a typical kalam closing formula.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the concise closing style.
2
Identify the key phrases
Identify the phrases 'weak reports' (أخبار ضعيفة) and 'similar ones exist on the side of Abu Bakr' (أمثالها موجودة في جانب أبي بكر).
Razi acknowledges both sides have weak reports.
3
Reflect on the purpose
Why does Razi mention that similar reports exist for Abu Bakr? This shows balance and avoids bias.
4
Understand the closing formula
The phrase 'والله أعلم' (God knows best) is a standard way to end a scholarly discussion, acknowledging that ultimate knowledge belongs to Allah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما بقية الوجوه فهي أخبار ضعيفة وأمثالها موجودة في جانب أبي بكر، على ما تشتمل عليه الكتب المصنفة في هذا الجنس. فهذا تمام الكلام في هذا الباب، والله أعلم.
As for the remaining proofs, they are weak reports, and similar ones exist on the side of Abu Bakr, as is contained in the books compiled on this topic. So this is the completion of the discussion in this chapter, and God knows best.
Source pages p. 467

Commentary
This passage is the concluding statement of the chapter. Razi summarily dismisses the remaining arguments for Ali's superiority as being based on weak reports, and notes that similar reports can be found supporting Abu Bakr. Thus, the chapter's discussion is closed. This is a typical kalam closing formula.

Key Terms
أخبار ضعيفة
بقية الوجوه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that Razi only dismisses arguments against his view without considering opposing evidence.
Correction: Razi explicitly states that similar weak reports exist in favor of Abu Bakr, showing balance.

Checkpoint

B) Weak reports with similar ones supporting Abu Bakr.
C) Based on consensus.
D) From the Qur'an.

Key Takeaway
Razi concludes the chapter by labeling remaining arguments as weak and balancing them with similar claims for Abu Bakr, then ends with a standard closing phrase.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In the source, Razi acknowledges that similar weak reports exist for Abu Bakr. When you debate or disagree with someone, do you honestly recognize that your own position may have weaknesses too, or do you only highlight the flaws of the other side? Will you commit to actively seeking your own blind spots today?
?
Razi closes by saying 'And God knows best.' When you reach the limits of your knowledge in a dispute, do you submit to God's ultimate knowledge, or do you insist on your own opinion beyond what evidence permits? What would it look like for you to genuinely trust that God knows best—starting with your next disagreement?

The Second Argument: Abu Bakr's Constant Jihad vs. Ali's Youth
Imam Razi presents and refutes the argument about jihad timing.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument that Abu Bakr's continuous struggle from the beginning of his Islam indicates his precedence, and how it is countered by considering Ali's youth and later jihad.

Core Idea
Abu Bakr's jihad began early, but Ali's came later—does timing determine virtue?

In this lesson, we examine the second argument presented in the debate over the precedence of Abu Bakr over Ali. The argument highlights Abu Bakr's constant struggle against disbelievers from the moment of his conversion, contrasting it with Ali's youth. We'll explore how this argument is constructed and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to grasp the overall flow. Then read the translation to understand the details.
Notice how the argument contrasts the roles of Abu Bakr and Ali.
2
Identify the key contrast
Look for the specific differences between Abu Bakr and Ali: Abu Bakr's continuous engagement vs. Ali's youth and late participation in jihad.
3
Understand the conclusion
The argument concludes that the beginning of jihad was for Abu Bakr and its end for Ali. Consider what this implies about their respective virtues.
This argument is one of several being refuted.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المسألة التاسعة والثلاثون
الثاني : أن أبا بكر لما أسلم كان أبدا في منازعة مع الكفار والمنافرة معهم، وبقي رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم ثلاث عشرة سنة في مكة ثم انتقل إلى المدينة، وبقي هناك سنة أخرى ثم نزلت آية القتال وأبو بكر في تلك المدة الطويلة في مكة والمدينة كان في الذب عن دين الله ومتابعة رسول الله الله وأما علي فإنه في ذلك الوقت كان صغير السن وما كان يخالط القوم، ثم إن بعد نزول آية القتال اشتغل علي هما بقتال الكفار. يفيد أن فثبت أن ابتداء الجهاد كان لأبي بكر، وآخره لعلي وأما الحجة الخامسة : وهي قولهم : إن إيمان علي سابق على إيمان أبي بكر. من الأحاد[؟] قلنا : وقد ذكرنا أن الأخبار فيه متعارضة وأخبارنا وإن كانت فكذا أخباركم وأيضًا فقد بينا أن إسلام أبي بكر أثر في قوة الإسلام أثرا عظيما، وما كان إسلام غيره كذلك. وأما الحجة السادسة : وهي التمسك بقصة خيبر - فجوابها : أن ذلك الكلام مجموع الصفات المذكورة في مدح الثاني غير حاصلة في مدح الأول، فلما قال : لأعطين الراية رجلًا يحب الله ورسوله ويحبه الله ورسوله، كرارًا غير فرار فهذا يدل على أن هذا المجموع ما كان حاصلا لأبي بكر وعمر ؛ لأن كونه غير فرار ما كان حاصلا فيهما، وكان ذلك المجموع غير حاصل فيهما، وعدم كونه كرارًا غير فرار لا في الفضيلة ألا ترى أن الأنبياء أفضل من الملائكة عند الشيعة، مع أنا نقطع أنه ليس للأنبياء من القدرة الحسية ذرة من القدرة التي للملائكة. نقصانا يوجب وأما بقية الوجوه فهي أخبار ضعيفة وأمثالها موجودة في جانب أبي بكر ، على ما تشتمل عليه الكتب المصنفة في هذا الجنس. فهذا تمام الكلام في هذا الباب، والله أعلم.
The thirty-ninth question. Second: That when Abu Bakr embraced Islam, he was constantly in conflict and opposition with the disbelievers. The Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) remained in Mecca for thirteen years, then migrated to Medina, and remained there another year, then the verse of fighting was revealed. And Abu Bakr during that long period in Mecca and Medina was defending the religion of Allah and following the Messenger of Allah. As for Ali, he was young at that time and did not mix with the people. Then after the revelation of the verse of fighting, Ali engaged with them in fighting the disbelievers. This indicates that the beginning of jihad was for Abu Bakr, and its end for Ali. As for the fifth argument: which is their statement that the faith of Ali preceded the faith of Abu Bakr from the hadiths. We said: we have mentioned that the reports on this are contradictory. Our reports, even if they are as they are, so are your reports. Also, we have shown that the Islam of Abu Bakr had a great effect on the strength of Islam, and the Islam of others was not like that. As for the sixth argument: which is holding fast to the story of Khaybar. Its answer is that that statement is a combination of attributes mentioned in praise of the latter (Ali) not found in praise of the former (Abu Bakr). When he said: 'I will certainly give the banner to a man who loves Allah and His Messenger, and Allah and His Messenger love him, one who attacks and does not flee' – this indicates that this combination was not present in Abu Bakr and Umar, because the quality of 'not fleeing' was not present in them, and that combination was not present in them. And the absence of being 'one who attacks and does not flee' does not cause a deficiency in virtue. Do you not see that the prophets are better than the angels according to the Shia, even though we are certain that the prophets do not have a speck of the sensory power that the angels have? It would cause a deficiency. As for the remaining arguments, they are weak hadiths, and their likes exist on the side of Abu Bakr, according to what is contained in the books compiled on this subject. This is the end of the discourse in this chapter. And Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 468

Commentary
This passage is a rebuttal of arguments concerning the precedence of the faith of Ali over Abu Bakr, within the context of the thirty-ninth question. The author first presents a second argument: that Abu Bakr engaged in constant struggle against disbelievers from the beginning of his Islam, while Ali was young and did not mix with the people until after the verse of fighting was revealed. Thus, the beginning of jihad is attributed to Abu Bakr and its end to Ali, implying Abu Bakr's earlier and more sustained effort. Next, the fifth argument is mentioned: the claim that Ali's faith preceded Abu Bakr's based on hadith. The author responds by stating that the reports are contradictory and that both sides have similar traditions. Moreover, the impact of Abu Bakr's Islam on strengthening the religion was immense, unlike that of others. Then the sixth argument is addressed: the story of Khaybar, where the Prophet said he would give the banner to one who loves Allah and His Messenger and who attacks and does not flee. The author explains that this combination of attributes (loving and being loved, attacking without fleeing) was not present in Abu Bakr and Umar, specifically the quality of 'not fleeing' was not present in them. However, the absence of this quality does not diminish virtue, just as prophets, though superior to angels, lack the sensory power of angels. The author concludes that the remaining arguments rely on weak hadiths with parallels on Abu Bakr's side, and then marks the end of the chapter.

Key Terms
أبو بكر
علي
جهاد
آية القتال
الذَب عن دين الله

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the argument, why is Abu Bakr considered to have initiated jihad?
A) Because he fought in all battles
B) Because he engaged in conflict from the start of his Islam, while Ali was young and did not fight until later
C) Because he led the Muslim army
D) Because he converted before Ali

Key Takeaway
The argument uses the timing of jihad to claim Abu Bakr's precedence, but it is only one of many considerations in the debate.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever judged your own faith journey by when you started practicing rather than the quality of your devotion? How does this argument—and its refutation—challenge you to reassess what truly matters to Allah?
?
Think of someone you know who converted to Islam later in life or became more committed after years of laxity. Does this lesson change how you view their efforts compared to those who grew up practicing? What concrete step can you take to honor their journey?
?
When you hear arguments that seem to prove someone's religious superiority based on external factors like age or timing, do you accept them uncritically? What in this lesson calls you to examine your own tendency to be impressed by such arguments? Repent of any hidden pride you may feel about your own early or consistent practice.
